Marrying the Sister of a Woman in 'Iddah: Hanafi vs. Opposing Schools
Ruling on marriage to sister of woman in 'iddah, with dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah from a bain divorce and evaluate the opposing schools' proofs.

Core Idea
A bain divorce does not sever all marital effects, so marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah remains prohibited.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi school and other schools regarding a specific marriage prohibition. The central question: can a man marry the sister of his ex-wife while she is observing 'iddah after an irrevocable (bain) divorce? The answer hinges on how one understands the legal effects of a bain divorce. The Hanafi school holds that several marital obligations persist, making the marriage akin to a revocable divorce in this context, and thus the sister remains prohibited. Other schools disagree, arguing the marriage bond is fully severed.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the exact ruling: It is prohibited (haram) for a man to marry the sister of a woman who is in 'iddah, even if the 'iddah is from a bain divorce.
2
Identify the Opposing Schools and Their Position
Name the schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Ibn Abi Layla) and their ruling: It is permissible to marry the sister in this case.
3
Follow the Hanafi Proof
Examine the Hanafi evidence: a report of Companion consensus and the reasoning that the marriage is still 'partially effective' — maintenance, housing, and the prohibition of remarriage continue.
The hadith about Companion agreement is cited, though the exact wording is disputed.
4
Analyze the Opponent's Proof
Understand the opposing argument: The marriage bond is completely severed; intercourse would incur the hadd penalty, so no marital effects remain.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis will not permit the marriage; Shafi'is and Malikis will permit it. Also note the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the umm walad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرم تزوج إلخ وقال الشافعي ومالك وابن أبي ليلى: يجوز أن يتزوج أخت المعتدة إذا كانت العدة من طلاق بائن أو ثلاث؛ لأن النكاح قد انقطع، ألا ترى أنه لو وطيها مع العلم بالحرمة يجب الحد، ولنا: ما روي أن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يجتمعوا على شيء كاجتماعهم على أربع قبل الظهر ، وأن لا تنكح امرأة في عدة أختها، وإمامنا فيه علي وابن مسعود وابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت له وكفا بهم قدوة؛ ولأن نكاح المطلقة قائم من وجه لبقاء أحكامه من النفقة، والسكنى، والمنع من الخروج، والفراش حتى ثبت نسب ولدها والقاطع أي الطلاق قد تأخر عمله في الأحكام غير حرمة الوطء، ولهذا بقي في حق القيد حتى لا يجوز لها أن تتزوج بغيره فصار كالرجعي، وعلى هذا الخلاف سائر محارمها وأربع سواها، وقالا يجوز نكاح أخت أم الولد في عدتها؛ لأن الحرمة لمكان الجمع نكاحاً يوجد، ولهذا جاز له أن يتزوج أربعاً سواها؛ ولأن العدة فيها أثر الملك والملك حقيقة فيها لا تمنع تزوج =
And it is prohibited to marry, etc. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Abi Layla said: It is permissible to marry the sister of a woman in 'iddah if the 'iddah is from a bain (irrevocable) or triple divorce; because the marriage has been severed. Do you not see that if he had intercourse with her knowing the prohibition, the hadd (penalty) would be obligatory? And our proof is what has been reported that the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) never agreed on anything like their agreement on four before Dhuhr, and that a woman should not be married during the 'iddah of her sister. And our imams in this are Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, and Zayd ibn Thabit, and they suffice as leaders. And because the marriage of the divorced woman remains in some respects due to the continuation of its rulings: maintenance, housing, prohibition of leaving, and the bed until the lineage of her child is established. And the separator (i.e., divorce) has delayed its effect in rulings other than the prohibition of intercourse. For this reason, it remains in restriction so that she cannot marry another, so it becomes like a raj'i (revocable) divorce. And upon this, the disagreement is regarding the rest of her mahrams and four others. And they both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible to marry the sister of a umm walad (mother of a child) during her 'iddah; because the prohibition is due to collection, which exists in marriage, and for this reason it is permissible for him to marry four others besides her. And because the 'iddah here has the effect of ownership, and ownership is real in her and does not prevent marriage. =
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah. The Hanafi school prohibits it even if the 'iddah is from a bain or triple divorce, because the marriage is still in effect for some rulings (like nafaqah, suknah, prohibition of remarriage). The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Ibn Abi Layla schools permit it because the marriage bond is severed. The Hanafi proof includes consensus of some companions. The text also mentions the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) regarding the umm walad: they permit marrying her sister during her 'iddah because the prohibition is only due to collection, and since the umm walad is in 'iddah due to the death of her master or his divorce, the ownership prevents full similarity to marriage. The text cuts off, but the gist is clear.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): A woman observing 'iddah (waiting period) after divorce or death.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a man marry the sister of his wife who is in 'iddah from a bain divorce?
A) Yes, because the marriage is completely severed.
B) No, because the marriage still has some effects.
C) Yes, only if the 'iddah is from a raj'i divorce.
D) No, for any type of 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a bain divorce does not entirely sever the marriage bond; thus the prohibition of marrying the sister continues throughout the 'iddah period.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Prohibition of Marrying Sister of Woman in 'Iddah
Ruling forbidding marrying sister of woman in 'iddah, with inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah, explain the companion consensus proof, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding umm walad.

Core Idea
A woman in 'iddah is still tied to the marriage; marrying her sister is forbidden.

In this passage, the Hanafi school asserts a general prohibition against marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah, whether the divorce is revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in/triple). The opposing schools—Shafi'i, Maliki, and Ibn Abi Layla—permit it in irrevocable divorces, arguing the marriage is completely severed. The Hanafis counter with a companion consensus that prohibits such marriages. Additionally, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding the umm walad (slave mother of a child), where Abu Hanifa forbids marrying her sister after emancipation, while his two disciples allow it during her 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling is that it is forbidden (haram) to marry the sister of a woman in any 'iddah, whether from a revocable or irrevocable divorce.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Abi Layla permit marrying the sister only when the 'iddah is from a ba'in or triple divorce. Their proof: the marriage is severed (e.g., hadd applies if intercourse occurs), so no prohibition remains.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof from companion consensus
The Hanafis cite a narration that the companions unanimously agreed that a woman should not be married while her sister is in 'iddah. This ijma is given as the primary dalil.
The narration: 'The Companions did not agree on anything as they agreed on four rak'ahs before Zuhr and that a woman should not be married while her sister is in 'iddah.'
4
Examine the Hanafi legal reasoning
Even in an irrevocable divorce, some marriage effects remain: maintenance, housing, prohibition of leaving, and the marriage bed for lineage. Thus the marriage is not fully severed, and the prohibition of collecting sisters applies as in a raj'i divorce.
5
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute over umm walad
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow marrying the sister of an umm walad during her 'iddah because the prohibition is due to joining in marriage, which can be limited by ownership. Abu Hanifa forbids it after emancipation because the marriage bed (firash) becomes strong, risking claiming lineage of two sisters' children simultaneously.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرم تزوج أخت المعتدة وقال الشافعي ومالك وابن أبي ليلى يجوز أن يتزوج أخت المعتدة إذا كانت العدة من طلاق بائن أو ثلاث لأن النكاح قد انقطع ألا ترى أنه لو وطيها مع العلم بالحرمة يجب الحد ولنا ما روي أن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يجتمعوا على شيء كاجتماعهم على أربع قبل الظهر وأن لا تنكح امرأة في عدة أختها وإمامنا فيه علي وابن مسعود وابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت وكفى بهم قدوة ولأن نكاح المطلقة قائم من وجه لبقاء أحكامه من النفقة والسكنى والمنع من الخروج والفراش حتى ثبت نسب ولدها والقاطع أي الطلاق قد تأخر عمله في الأحكام غير حرمة الوطء ولهذا بقي في حق القيد حتى لا يجوز لها أن تتزوج بغيره فصار كالرجعي وعلى هذا الخلاف سائر محارمها وأربع سواها وقالا يجوز نكاح أخت أم الولد في عدتها لأن الحرمة لمكان الجمع نكاحاً يوجد ولهذا جاز له أن يتزوج أربعاً سواها ولأن العدة فيها أثر الملك والملك حقيقة فيها لا تمنع تزوج الأخت فالأثر أولى ولأبي حنيفة أنه إنما جاز نكاح أخت أم الولد لضعف الفراش فإذا أعتقها قوي الفراش ولهذا لا يجوز تزويجها بعد العتق حتى تنقضي عدتها وقبله يجوز فإذا قوي الفراش لا يجوز له أن يتزوج أختها كيلا يكون مستلحقاً نسب ولد أختين في زمان واحد بخلاف أربع سواها لعدم هذا المعنى
And it is forbidden to marry the sister of a woman in 'iddah. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Abi Layla said: It is permissible to marry the sister of a woman in 'iddah if the 'iddah is from an irrevocable (ba'in) or triple divorce, because the marriage has been severed; do you not see that if he had intercourse with her knowing the prohibition, the hadd penalty would be obligatory. Our proof: what has been narrated that the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not unanimously agree on anything as they unanimously agreed on the four prayers before Zuhr and that a woman should not be married while her sister is in 'iddah. And our imams in this are 'Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Abbas, and Zayd ibn Thabit, and they suffice as role models. And because the marriage of the divorced woman is in some respects ongoing, as its rulings remain: maintenance, housing, prohibition of leaving, and the marriage bed until the lineage of her child is established. And the cutter (i.e., divorce) has delayed its effect in other rulings except the prohibition of intercourse, and for this reason she remains restricted such that she cannot marry another, so it becomes like a revocable (raj'i) divorce. And this khilaf applies to all her other relatives and four wives besides her. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible to marry the sister of an umm walad (mother of a child) during her 'iddah, because the prohibition is due to joining in marriage, which exists, and for this reason it is permissible for him to marry four women besides her, and because the 'iddah here has an effect of ownership, and ownership in reality does not prevent marrying the sister. So the effect is more appropriate. Abu Hanifa said: It is only permissible to marry the sister of an umm walad due to the weakness of the marriage bed (firash). When he frees her, the marriage bed becomes strong, and for this reason after the emancipation it is not permissible to marry her until her 'iddah expires, whereas before the emancipation it is permissible. So when the marriage bed becomes strong, it is not permissible for him to marry her sister, lest he claim the lineage of the children of two sisters in one time, unlike four other women, because this meaning is absent.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that it is forbidden to marry the sister of a woman in 'iddah, whether the 'iddah is from a revocable or irrevocable divorce. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ibn Abi Layla permit it only in case of ba'in or triple divorce, arguing the marriage is severed. The Hanafis rebut using the companion agreement that a woman should not be married while her sister is in 'iddah and that the divorced marriage retains some effects. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is discussed: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow marrying the sister of an umm walad during her 'iddah, but Abu Hanifa forbids it after emancipation because the firash becomes strong and would lead to claiming lineage of two sisters' children simultaneously. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa: it is forbidden to marry the sister of a woman in 'iddah in all cases, including umm walad after her emancipation.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): A woman in her waiting period ('iddah) after divorce or death.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is it forbidden to marry the sister of an umm walad after she is emancipated?
A) Because the umm walad becomes like a free woman and her 'iddah is longer.
B) Because the marriage bed (firash) becomes strong, and marrying her sister would lead to claiming lineage of children of two sisters at the same time.
C) Because the umm walad's 'iddah is not valid after emancipation.
D) Because the sister of an umm walad is always related by blood.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits marrying the sister of a woman in 'iddah, whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable, based on companion consensus and the ongoing effects of marriage.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
8 min
Prohibition of Marrying One's Slave Woman or Female Master
Ruling statement with talil (causal reasoning)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a marriage between a master and his slave woman (or a slave and his female master) is batil and explain the talil (causal reasoning) based on the nature of milk al-mut'a.

Core Idea
A marriage contract is void if its essential effect already exists by prior ownership.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a ruling that is both clear and instructive in Hanafi legal reasoning. It does not simply state a prohibition but explains why these marriages are invalid: because the purpose of marriage—ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'a)—is already present in one direction. Understanding this causal argument (talil) trains you to think like a Hanafi jurist, linking rulings to their underlying rationales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited marriages
The text rules against a master marrying his own slave woman, and a slave marrying his own female master. Note that these are distinct scenarios with separate lines of reasoning.
Both cases are discussed under the same heading but require different arguments.
2
Analyze the purpose of marriage (milk al-mut'a)
Recognize that in Hanafi jurisprudence, the primary purpose of marriage is to establish 'ownership of enjoyment' (milk al-mut'a)—the lawful right to sexual relations between spouses.
This is a key concept specific to Hanafi nikah theory.
3
Apply the principle that a cause must produce a new effect
The text argues: if the effect (milk al-mut'a) already exists by virtue of ownership (master owns the slave woman), then the marriage contract would be 'establishing what already exists,' which is legally futile. A valid cause must produce a new result.
This is a logical maxim: something that exists cannot be re-established.
4
Distinguish between legal invalidity and sin (punishment)
The passage explicitly clarifies that 'prohibition' here refers to the contract being void (batil), not necessarily to sin deserving punishment. This is a subtle but important distinction in Hanafi legal theory.
The contract is invalid ab initio; no rights or obligations arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمته وسيدته أي حرم أن يتزوج السيد أمته أو العبد سيدته أما تزوج الأمة فلأن المقصود من النكاح إثبات ملك المتعة وهو ثابت للمولى قبل النكاح فيلزم إثبات الثابت والسبب إذا لم يفد حكمه يلغو وأما تزوج العبد سيدته فلأن المرأة مالكته فلا يتحقق كونها مملوكة لأن المملوكية ينافي المالكية وليس المراد من حرمة التزوج بأمته استحقاق العقوبة بل المراد في أحكام النكاح من ثبوت المهر في ذمة المولى وبقاء النكاح بعد الإعتاق ووقوع الطلاق عليها وغير ذلك والحرمة في هذين العقدين بالإجماع على بطلانهما
And his slave woman and his female master: meaning that it is forbidden for a master to marry his slave woman, or a slave to marry his female master. As for marrying a slave woman: because the purpose of marriage is to establish ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'a), and that is already established for the master before marriage, so it would be establishing what already exists, and if a cause does not yield its ruling, it becomes void. As for a slave marrying his female master: because the woman is his owner, so she cannot be owned, since ownership contradicts being owned. And what is meant by the prohibition of marrying one's slave woman is not deserving of punishment, but rather it refers to the rulings of marriage: the obligation of mahr on the master's wealth, the continuation of marriage after emancipation, the occurrence of divorce on her, and other things. And the prohibition in these two contracts is by consensus that they are invalid.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This is a ruling statement that it is forbidden (haram) and invalid for a master to marry his own slave woman, and for a slave to marry his own female master. The reasoning is based on the purpose of marriage (milk al-mut'a) already being vested in the master, and for a slave, ownership cannot be established because the woman is the owner. The prohibition here is not in terms of sin (since there is no legal capacity in some cases) but rather that the contract is void and produces no legal effects. There is consensus on this ruling.

Key Terms
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'a): Ownership of enjoyment, i.e., the right to have sexual relations with one's spouse, which is the purpose of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal status of a marriage contract between a man and his own slave woman according to the Hanafi school?
A) Valid but reprehensible (makruh).
B) Invalid (batil) by consensus.
C) Valid if the man owns her with right hand possession.
D) Valid after she gives consent.

Key Takeaway
A marriage contract is void if its essential effect (milk al-mut'a) already exists through ownership, or if the parties cannot be both owner and owned.

Marrying Non-Muslim Women: Magians, Idol Worshippers, Kitabiyya, and Sabians
Rulings on marrying non-Muslim women: Magians and idol worshippers prohibited, Kitabiyya and Sabians permitted (with dispute).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on marrying Magian, idol worshipper, Kitabiyya, and Sabian women, citing primary evidence, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding Sabians.

Core Idea
Magians and idol worshippers are forbidden; Kitabiyya are permitted; Sabians are disputed within the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines the rulings on marrying women of different faiths. While the Quran and hadith clearly prohibit marriage to polytheists, the status of Kitabiyya (Scriptuaries) is allowed. The Sabians present an interesting intra-Hanafi dispute based on whether they are considered idol worshippers or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Identify the four groups: Magian (مجوسية), idol worshipper (وثنية), Kitabiyya (كتابية), and Sabian (صابية). Note the ruling for each as stated in the passage.
The passage lists them in sequence.
2
State the ruling and evidence for Magians
Magian women are forbidden. The evidence is the hadith instructing to treat them like People of the Book but not marry their women.
Tirmidhi and others recorded this hadith.
3
State the ruling and evidence for idol worshippers
Idol worshippers are forbidden based on Quran 2:221. The passage then addresses an objection that this verse might include Kitabiyyat.
Two arguments are given to exclude Kitabiyyat: takhsis by Quran 5:5 and the linguistic point that ‘mushrik’ is distinct from ‘Ahl al-Kitab’.
4
State the ruling for Kitabiyya
Kitabiyya (Jews and Christians, and others with revealed scriptures) are permissible based on Quran 5:5. The passage extends this to any scriptuary, contrary to Shafi'i.
The passage also cites the practice of Hudhayfah marrying a Jewish woman.
5
Analyze the Sabian dispute
Sabians: Abu Hanifa permits; Sahibayn forbid. The dispute hinges on whether Sabians worship stars (making them idol worshippers) or merely venerate them (like Muslims venerate the Kaaba). The passage notes that if Abu Hanifa's interpretation is correct, there is consensus on permissibility.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوسية والوثنية والمجوسية أي وحرم أيضاً تزوج المجوسية وهي من لا دين لها ولا كتاب وأما حرمة تزوج المجوسية فلقوله عليه "سنوا بهم سنة أهل الكتاب غير ناكحي نسائهم ولا آكلي ذبائحهم" وأما حرمة الوثنية فلقوله تعالى {ولا تنكحوا المشركات حتى يؤمن} فإن قيل قال الله تعالى {وقالت اليهود عزيز ابن الله وقالت النصارى المسيح ابن الله} فيتناول الآية نساء أهل الكتاب أيضاً فما وجه التخصيص بالمجوسية والوثنية قلنا فيه وجهان الأول أن الكتابيات خصصن من هذا العموم بقوله تعالى {والمحصنات من الذين أوتوا الكتاب} فبقيت في ما وراءهن على عمومها والثاني أن اسم المشرك لا يتناول أهل الكتاب مطلقاً لأن الله تعالى عطف المشركين على أهل الكتاب في قوله {لم يكن الذين كفروا من أهل الكتاب والمشركين} والعطف يقتضي المغايرة وكل مذهب يكفر معتقده فهو يحرم نكاحها لأن اسم المشرك يتناولهم جميعا والوثنية وكذا تزوج الوثنية وهي من تعبد الأصنام لقوله تعالى {ولا تنكحوا المشركات حتى يؤمن} وحل تزوج الكتابية لقوله تعالى {والمحصنات من الذين أوتوا الكتاب من قبلكم} وعن ابن عمر أنه لا يحل لأنها مشركة لأنهم يعبدون المسيح وعزيراً ومعنى الآية التي أسلمن من أهل الكتاب لكن لا لتخصيص الكتابية فإن المجوسية والوثنية إذا أسلمت حل نكاحها وقد صح أن حذيفة تزوج يهودية وكل من يعتقد ديناً سماوياً وله كتاب منزل كصحف إبراهيم وشيث وزبور داود فهم من أهل الكتاب فيجوز مناكحتهم وأكل ذبائحهم خلافاً للشافعي فيما عدا اليهود والنصارى وقوله "والصابية" أي حل تزوج الصابية عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما وهذا مبني على أنهم يعبدون النجوم فهم عبدة الأوثان عندهما لأنهم يعبدون النجوم وعنده ليس كذلك وإنما هم يعظمون النجوم كتعظيم المسلم الكعبة فإن كان الأمر كما فسره يجوز المناكحة بالإجماع
And the Magian and the idol worshipper: the Magian meaning it is also forbidden to marry a Magian woman, who is one with no religion and no book. As for the prohibition of marrying the Magian, it is due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Treat them like the People of the Book, but do not marry their women nor eat their slaughtered meat.' As for the prohibition of the idol worshipper, it is due to the saying of Allah Most High: {And do not marry the polytheist women until they believe} (2:221). If it is said: Allah said {And the Jews said 'Uzayr is the son of Allah, and the Christians said the Messiah is the son of Allah} (9:30), and this verse includes the women of the People of the Book as well, so what is the basis for specifying the Magian and the idol worshipper? We say: there are two aspects. First, that the Kitabiyyat are excluded from this generality by His saying {And chaste women from those who were given the Book before you} (5:5), so those besides them remain under the generality. Second, that the name 'mushrik' (polytheist) does not include the People of the Book absolutely, because Allah Most High coordinated the mushrikin with the People of the Book in His saying {Those who disbelieved among the People of the Book and the polytheists} (98:1), and coordination requires differentiation. And every sect that declares its follower an unbeliever, then marrying her is forbidden, because the name 'mushrik' includes all of them. And the idol worshipper: similarly, marrying the idol worshipper (al-wathaniyya), who worships idols, is forbidden due to the saying of Allah Most High {And do not marry the polytheist women until they believe}. And it is permissible to marry the Kitabiyya (woman from the People of the Book) due to His saying {And chaste women from those who were given the Book before you}. Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) reported from him that it is not permissible because she is a polytheist, as they worship the Messiah and 'Uzayr. And the meaning of the verse is that those who become Muslims from the People of the Book [are permissible], not that the Kitabiyya is specified. But if the Magian or idol worshipper becomes Muslim, her marriage becomes permissible. And it is authenticated that Hudhayfah (may Allah be pleased with him) married a Jewish woman. And everyone who believes in a revealed religion and has a revealed book, like the scriptures of Ibrahim, Shith, and the Zabur of Dawud, they are from the People of the Book, so it is permissible to marry them and eat their slaughtered meat, contrary to al-Shafi'i for other than Jews and Christians. And his saying: 'and the Sabian (al-Sabi'a)' meaning it is permissible to marry the Sabian according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And this is based on whether they worship the stars, thus they are idol worshippers according to the two, because they worship the stars, but according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is not so; rather they venerate the stars like a Muslim venerate the Ka'bah. If the matter is as he explained, then marrying them is permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings on marrying non-Muslim women. Magian women (fire worshippers) are forbidden based on a hadith directing to treat them like People of the Book but not marry them. Idol worshippers are forbidden by the Quranic verse {And do not marry polytheist women}. An objection that the verse also includes Kitabiyyat is refuted by two arguments: (1) Kitabiyyat are excluded by Quran 5:5; (2) the term 'mushrik' is distinct from 'Ahl al-Kitab' in Quran 98:1. Then it states the permissibility of marrying Kitabiyya (Jews and Christians) based on Quran 5:5, citing the practice of Hudhayfah, and extending to other scriptuaries (like followers of scriptures of Ibrahim, etc.) contrary to Shafi'i. Finally, it discusses Sabians (star worshippers): Abu Hanifa permits marrying them because they venerate stars, not worship them, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad forbid it. If Abu Hanifa's interpretation is correct, there is consensus on permissibility.

Key Terms
المجوسية (al-majusiyya): A Magian woman; one who follows the religion of Zoroastrianism, which has a scripture but is not considered from the People of the Book for marriage.
الصابية (al-sabi'a): Sabian woman; a follower of a religion that venerates the stars; considered by Abu Hanifa as permissible to marry because they are not idol worshippers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi prohibition of marrying a Magian woman?
A) Quranic verse explicitly mentioning Magians.
B) A hadith instructing to treat them like People of the Book but not marry their women.
C) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions.
D) Analogy to idol worshippers.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of marrying a non-Muslim woman in the Hanafi school depends on whether she is from the People of the Book (Kitabiyya) or not, with a dispute regarding Sabians.

The Initial List of Prohibited and Permissible Women in Marriage
Matn listing prohibited and permissible women categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of permanently prohibited women and two categories of permissible women in marriage according to the Hanafi school, as listed in the matn.

Core Idea
Five categories of women are permanently prohibited; two are permissible, including Kitabiyya and Sabiyya.

In this opening passage of the chapter on prohibited women, the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq provides a concise memorization anchor. It lists women whom a man may never marry, as well as two categories whose marriage is permitted. This lesson introduces these categories before the commentary expands on details and disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five prohibited categories
Read the matn and list the five types of women mentioned as prohibited: mu'taddatuhu, amatuhu, sayyidatuhu, al-majusiyya, al-wathaniyya.
These are permanent prohibitions due to various reasons: existing marriage ties, master-servant relationship, or religion.
2
Identify the two permissible categories
The matn also lists two permissible types: al-kitabiyya and al-sabiyya. Note the parenthetical definition of sabiyya as one who worships angels.
The permissibility of Kitabiyya is agreed upon; Sabiyya is disputed.
3
Classify the prohibitions as permanent or temporary
All five prohibitions mentioned are permanent (mu'abbad) in the sense that they are prohibited as long as the condition exists. However, some prohibitions like a woman in 'iddah are temporary until her 'iddah ends. Understand the context.
The matn lists them as a group; commentary clarifies nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المحرمات معتدته وأمته وسيّدته والمجوسية والوثنية، وحلّ تزوج الكتابية والصابية وهي من يعبد الملائكة
Chapter on prohibited women: his wife in 'iddah, his slave-girl, his female master, the Magian woman, and the idolatress; and the permissibility of marrying the Kitabiyya and the Sabiyya, and she is one who worships the angels.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This is the opening matn (memorization anchor) of the chapter on prohibited women (muharramat) in the book of Nikah. It lists five categories of women who are permanently forbidden in marriage: (1) a woman in her 'iddah (waiting period) from a previous marriage, (2) one's own slave-girl (umm walad or otherwise), (3) one's own female master (if the man is a slave), (4) a Magian woman (Majusiyya), and (5) an idolatrous woman (Wathaniyya). It then mentions two categories of women whose marriage is permissible: the Kitabiyya (a woman from the People of the Book, i.e., Jewish or Christian) and the Sabiyya (Sabian). The text parenthetically defines the Sabiyya as one who worships the angels. This definition will be the subject of scholarly dispute later in the commentary.

Key Terms
معتدته (mu‘taddatuhu): A woman who is in her waiting period ('iddah) due to divorce or death of her husband, and thus prohibited for marriage to another man during that period.
المجوسية (al-majūsiyya): A woman who follows the Magian religion (Zoroastrianism), considered to have no divine book or recognized religion.
الصابية (al-ṣābi’a): A Sabian woman, defined here as one who worships angels. The ruling on marriage to her is disputed within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A Kitabiyya woman
B) A Majusiyya woman
C) A Sabiyya woman
D) A free believing woman

Key Takeaway
The matn provides a foundational list of five permanently prohibited and two permissible women in marriage; all other women are generally permissible unless a specific prohibition is established.

Marrying the Sister of One's Umm Walad: Before and After Freeing
Ruling on marrying sister of umm walad with proof based on firash.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying the sister of one's umm walad after freeing her, and explain the legal reasoning based on the strength of the marital bond (firash).

Core Idea
Marrying the sister of one's umm walad is permitted before freeing her but becomes prohibited after freeing due to the strengthened firash.

This lesson examines a nuanced case within the 'iddah rulings: the sister of one's umm walad. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the period before and after freeing the umm walad, based on the concept of firash. The lesson explores the ta'lil and its implications for the permissibility of marrying the sister.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying the sister of one's umm walad. The ruling changes based on whether the umm walad has been freed and her 'iddah status.
Before freeing: permissible. After freeing: impermissible until 'iddah ends.
2
Locate the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Abu Hanifa argues that the permissibility before freeing is due to the weakness of the marital bond (firash). After freeing, the bond strengthens, making it akin to a free wife, thus prohibiting marrying the sister.
The key concept is the strength of firash; a weak bond does not create a full marital impediment.
3
Apply the ruling to the sister of umm walad
If a man frees his umm walad, he may not marry her sister until the umm walad's 'iddah expires. This is to avoid claiming lineage of two sisters' children at one time.
Contrast with four free wives: no such issue arises because the bond is already strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأخت فالأثر ،أولى، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه إنما جاز نكاح أخت أم الولد لضعف الفراش، فإذا أعتقها قوي الفراش، ولهذا لا يجوز تزويجها بعد العتق حتى تنقضي عدتها، وقبله يجوز فإذا قوي الفراش لا يجوز له أن يتزوج أختها؛ كيلا يكون مستلحقاً نسب ولد أختين في زمان واحد بخلاف أربع سواها، لعدم هذا المعنى. (فتح، عيني) أو بائن أو ثلاث أو نكاح فاسد أو شبهته. (ط) معتدته من رجعي
The sister, then the effect, is more appropriate. And according to Abu Hanifa: the permissibility of marrying the sister of the umm walad [is] due to the weakness of the marital bond (firash). When he frees her, the bond strengthens, and therefore it is not permissible to marry her [the sister] after the freeing until her 'iddah expires, whereas before it is permissible. When the bond strengthens, it is not permissible for him to marry her sister, lest he claim lineage of the child of two sisters at one time, unlike four [free women] besides her, due to the absence of this meaning. (Fath, 'Ayni) Or [if the 'iddah is] from a ba'in [divorce], or three [divorces], or a fasid marriage, or its shubha. (T) His [the woman in] 'iddah of a raj'i [divorce].
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage delves into a specific sub-ruling within the general prohibition of marrying a woman in her 'iddah. The commentary explains the legal reasoning (ta'lil) of Abu Hanifa regarding a man who owns a slave-girl who has borne his child (umm walad). He is permitted to marry her sister before he frees the umm walad because the marital bond (firash) is weak. Once he frees her, the bond strengthens (now she is like a free wife), and he cannot marry her sister until the umm walad's 'iddah expires. The reasoning is to avoid the possibility of claiming lineage of two sisters' children at the same time (which could cause conflict in paternity). This is contrasted with the case of four free wives, where that problem does not arise. The passage then lists the types of 'iddah to which this ruling applies: ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, three divorces, a fasid (invalid) marriage, or its shubha (semblance). Finally, it mentions specifically the 'iddah of a raj'i (revocable) divorce as a subcategory.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master, thereby acquiring a special status that restricts her sale and gives her certain rights.
الفراش (al-firāsh): The marital bond or bed that establishes paternity. Here, weak firash refers to the umm walad's status before freeing.
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is it permissible to marry the sister of one's umm walad before freeing the umm walad?
A) Because the umm walad is not a free wife
B) Because the marital bond (firash) is weak
C) Because the man is not considered married to the umm walad
D) Because there is no risk of lineage confusion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on marrying the sister of one's umm walad hinges on the strength of the marital bond (firash): before freeing it is weak and permissible, after freeing it is strong and prohibited until the 'iddah ends, to prevent lineage confusion.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Prohibition of Marrying One's Own Slave-Girl or Master
Expansion of two prohibited marriages with legal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal reasoning for the prohibition of a master marrying his slave-girl and a slave marrying his female master, and identify the practical consequences of such void contracts.

Core Idea
Marriage to one's own slave is void because the right to sexual enjoyment is already owned—establishing the already established is meaningless.

Marriage transfers ownership of sexual enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah). But what if you already own the person? The Hanafi school, using logical reasoning (ta'lil), explains why such contracts are inherently void. This lesson unpacks the two prohibitions from the matn and the legal principles behind them, emphasizing that the invalidity is not merely a matter of punishment but has concrete effects on marriage rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited marriages
Locate the two cases: a master marrying his own slave-girl, and a slave marrying his own female master. Note that the prohibition is by consensus (ijma).
The text explicitly states both prohibitions.
2
Understand the purpose of marriage (milk al-mut'ah)
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, the primary purpose of marriage is to establish ownership of sexual enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah). This right is transferred from the wife to the husband via the marriage contract.
This is a key Hanafi concept.
3
Apply the principle of ithbat al-thabit (establishing the already established)
For the master marrying his slave-girl: he already owns milk al-mut'ah over her through ownership. The marriage contract would attempt to establish something already existing, which is logically invalid. A cause (the contract) that does not produce a new ruling is void.
This reasoning is based on qiyas (analogy) and usul al-fiqh.
4
Recognize the contradiction of ownership and ownedness
For the slave marrying his female master: the woman is the owner, so she cannot simultaneously be owned. Ownership and ownedness are mutually exclusive. Therefore, the contract cannot fulfill the purpose of marriage.
This is a direct logical contradiction.
5
List the practical consequences of void contracts
If such a contract were made, it would not produce valid marriage effects: no dower (mahr) is owed, no inheritance, no divorce (talaq) that counts, etc. The prohibition is not just for punishment but has real fiqhi implications.
The text emphasizes these effects to differentiate from mere sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمته وسيدته: أي حرم أن يتزوج السيد أمته أو العبد سيدته، أما تزوج الأمة؛ فلأن المقصود من النكاح إثبات ملك المتعة، وهو ثابت للمولى قبل النكاح فيلزم إثبات الثابت، والسبب إذا لم يفد حكمه يلغو، وأما تزوج العبد سيدته فلان المرأة مالكته فلا يتحقق كونها مملوكة؛ لأن المملوكية ينافي المالكية، وليس المراد من حرمة التزوج بأمته استحقاق العقوبة، بل المراد في أحكام النكاح من ثبوت المهر في ذمة المولى، وبقاء النكاح بعد الإعتاق ووقوع الطلاق عليها وغير ذلك، والحرمة في هذين العقدين بالإجماع على بطلانهما. (فتح، مستخلص)
And his slave-girl and his female master: i.e., it is prohibited for a master to marry his slave-girl or for a slave to marry his female master. As for marrying the slave-girl, because the purpose of marriage is to establish ownership of sexual enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), and that is already established for the master before marriage, so it would be establishing something already established, and a cause that does not produce its ruling is invalid. As for the slave marrying his female master, because the woman is his owner, so her being owned is not realized, since ownedness contradicts ownership. The intended meaning of the prohibition of marrying one's slave-girl is not [merely] deserving punishment, but rather in the rulings of marriage: such as the establishment of dower in the master's liability, the continuity of marriage after manumission, the occurrence of divorce on her, etc. And the prohibition in these two contracts is by consensus on their invalidity. (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage expands on two of the prohibited categories from the matn: marrying one's own slave-girl (amah) and marrying one's female master (sayyidah). The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the essential purpose of marriage is to establish milk al-mut'ah (ownership of sexual enjoyment). In the case of a master marrying his own slave-girl, he already possesses this right through ownership, so the contract would be establishing something already existent, which is invalid (yulzim ithbat al-thabit). In the case of a slave marrying his owner, the woman is the owner, so she cannot simultaneously be owned; ownership and ownedness are contradictory. The prohibition is not just a matter of punishment but has practical consequences in the rulings of marriage: if such a contract were made, it would not create valid marriage effects like dower (mahr) and divorce (talaq). The text emphasizes that these contracts are invalid by consensus (ijma).

Key Terms
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut‘ah): The right to sexual enjoyment, which is the primary purpose of marriage according to Hanafi fiqh.
إثبات الثابت (ithbāt al-thābit): A logical fallacy: establishing something that already exists, rendering the action meaningless and invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave's marriage to his female master invalid according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because a slave cannot own property
B) Because the woman is his owner, and ownership and ownedness cannot coexist
C) Because the master would not pay dower
D) Because it is prohibited by consensus, but no reason is given

Key Takeaway
Marrying one's own slave is void by consensus because it attempts to establish a right that already exists or is logically impossible.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage in Ihram: Validity and the Shafi'i Objection
Marriage in ihram: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage in ihram and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-evidence using the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas.

Core Idea
The Prophet married Maimunah in ihram — Hanafis accept; Shafi'i's hadith is reinterpreted as referring to intercourse.

One of the prohibitions of ihram is engaging in marital relations, but does the contract itself become invalid? The Hanafi school, based on the practice of the Prophet, holds that marriage in ihram is valid. This lesson examines the primary evidence and the Shafi'i objection, along with the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is contracting marriage while one or both parties are in the state of ihram. The Hanafi ruling: it is valid (sahih).
2
Locate the Shafi'i dalil
Shafi'i cites the hadith: 'The muhrim should not marry nor be married' (or 'should not propose'). This is a direct prohibition.
Imam al-Shafi'i takes the apparent meaning as general prohibition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet married Maimunah while in ihram is stronger (narrated by Muslim); (2) the Shafi'i hadith is weak according to Bukhari, or if authentic, it refers to intercourse (the primary literal meaning of 'nikah' in the hadith).
The metaphor of 'marriage' for intercourse is common in Arabic.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The narration of Yazid ibn al-Asmm that the Prophet married Maimunah while halal is not strong enough to contradict Ibn 'Abbas. Alternatively, 'marriage' in that report may mean 'consummation'.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person marries while in ihram, the Hanafi school says the marriage is valid; the Shafi'i school says it is invalid and must be renewed after ihram.
This affects the validity of the contract and any children born from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط) لا عكسه: [ أي لا يحل نكاح الأمة على الحرة. (ع)] وهو تزوج الأمة على الحرة؛ لقوله عليه: "لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة"، وقال الشافعي: يجوز ذلك للعبد، وقال مالك: يجوز برضاء الحرة، والحجة عليهما ما روينا رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۷/۱] ولو في عدة الحرة: [سواء كانت عدة طلاق بائن أو رجعي أي لا يجوز تزوج الأمة على الحرة وإن كان في عدة الحرة. (ط،ع)] متصل بما قبله أي لا يجوز تزوج الأمة على الحرة وإن كانت في عدة الحرة عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يجوز إن كانت العدة من طلاق بائن؛ لأن الحرام إدخال الأمة على الحرة، وهذه مباينة، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله: أن النكاح باق من وجه كنكاح الأخت في عدة .الأخت. And the woman in ihram even if she is in the state of ihram: i.e., it is permissible to marry a woman in ihram even if the husband or the arranging guardian is in ihram. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The muhrim (one in ihram) should not marry and should not be married,' and in another version: 'should not propose' narrated by Muslim. Our proof: the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that he (peace be upon him) married Maimunah while he was in ihram, narrated by Muslim and others. And what he (Shafi'i) narrated is weak, according to al-Bukhari. And even if it is authentic, it is interpreted as referring to intercourse, because that is the literal meaning: i.e., the muhrim should not have intercourse and the muhrima should not allow intercourse. The masculine form (la yankih) is considering the person. If you say: Yazid ibn al-Asamm narrated that he married her while he was halal, I say: his narration does not contradict the narration of Ibn 'Abbas, and it is for this reason that 'Amr ibn Dinar al-Zuhri said: 'Does Ibn al-Asamm know? He is a Bedouin who urinates on his leg, do you make him like Ibn 'Abbas?' Or it is interpreted as meaning that by 'marriage' he intended the wedding night (bina') metaphorically, because it is its cause, so it is permissible to apply it to the wedding night. And His statement: 'and the slave-girl even if she is Kitabiyya' – this is according to us (Hanafis). Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible for a free man to marry a Kitabiyya slave-girl, subject to the condition of inability to marry a free woman, and [if he does] he marries a Muslim [slave-girl] due to the verse: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then [let him marry] from what your right hands possess' (al-Nisa' 4:25); he permitted marriage to slave-girls with two conditions: lack of ability and that she be believing. Our proof is the verse: 'Marry what seems good to you of women' (al-Nisa' 4:3) which is general and includes both slave-girls and free women. What follows establishes the ruling when the described condition is present, when the condition of inability exists, but does not negate or affirm its absence. (T) Not the opposite: i.e., it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl while having a free wife. (A) And that is marrying a slave-girl on top of a free wife, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Do not marry a slave-girl on top of a free woman.' Al-Shafi'i said: that is permissible for a slave [to do thus], and Malik said: it is permissible with the consent of the free woman. The proof against them is what we have narrated. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:197) And even if [the free wife] is in her 'iddah: [whether the 'iddah is from a ba'in or raj'i divorce, i.e., it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl on top of a free wife even if she (the free woman) is in her 'iddah. (T, A)] It is connected to the previous: i.e., it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl on top of a free wife even if the free woman is in her 'iddah, according to Abu Hanifah. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is permissible if the 'iddah is from a ba'in divorce, because the prohibition is [due to] bringing the slave-girl into the same household as the free woman, and this [case] is separate. And the three [schools?] also said this. But he (Abu Hanifah) has: that the marriage [of the free woman] remains in some respects, like the marriage of a sister during the 'iddah of another sister. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:197) And four from among free women etc., due to the verse: 'Marry what seems good to you of women: two, three, or four' (al-Nisa' 4:3). Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible from slave-girls except one, because its permissibility is out of necessity and that necessity is satisfied with one. And the proof against him is what we have recited. And His statement: 'only' indicates that it is not permissible to marry more than four, and there is consensus of the Ummah on that. Al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim said: it is permissible to marry nine...
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This final passage covers three distinct rulings with inter-madhhab disputes. First: the validity of marriage while in the state of ihram (hajj/umrah). The Hanafi position, based on the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maimunah while in ihram, is that such marriage is valid. Al-Shafi'i rejects this, citing another hadith 'la yankih al-muhrim' (the one in ihram should not marry). The Hanafi response is that the latter hadith is weak, and if authentic, it refers to intercourse, not the marriage contract. Second: the permissibility of a free man marrying a slave-girl, even a Kitabiyya one. Hanafis uphold general permissibility based on the verse 'Marry what seems good to you of women' (4:3), while Shafi'i restricts it to cases of inability to marry a free woman and requires the slave-girl to be Muslim. Hanafis argue the general verse is not restricted by the condition of inability; the condition (in verse 4:25) is for a specific situation and does not negate permissibility otherwise. Third: the prohibition of marrying a slave-girl while already having a free wife (collecting them). Hanafis absolutely prohibit this, citing the hadith 'la tankih al-amah 'ala al-hurrah'. Shafi'i allows it for a slave, and Malik allows it with the free wife's consent. The Hanafi evidence is from hadith. An edge case is when the free wife is in her 'iddah: Abu Hanifah maintains the prohibition, while his two companions permit it if the 'iddah is from a ba'in divorce, because the free wife's marital bond is completely severed (they are 'separate'). Finally, the passage touches on the maximum number of wives: four for free women, with consensus that no more than four are allowed (refuting the view of al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim who allowed nine).

Key Terms
المحرم (al-muḥrim): One who is in the state of ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah, during which certain acts are prohibited.
لا ينكح المحرم (lā yankih al-muḥrim): A hadith that literally means 'the muhrim should not marry' but is interpreted by Hanafis as referring to intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidential basis for the Hanafi position that marriage in ihram is valid?
A) The Quranic verse 4:3 'Marry what seems good to you'
B) The hadith 'La yankih al-muhrim' interpreted loosely
C) The practice of the Prophet marrying Maimunah while in ihram
D) Consensus of the companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates marriage in ihram based on the Prophet's own example, overruling a weak or interpretable prohibition hadith.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marrying a Slave-Girl: General Permissibility vs. Shafi'i Conditions
Marrying a slave-girl: Hanafi permissibility vs. Shafi'i conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a free man may marry any slave-girl, even a Kitabiyya, and refute the Shafi'i conditional view.

Core Idea
The Quranic general command 'Marry what seems good' includes slave-girls; later conditions do not restrict unless proven.

Marriage to a slave-girl raises questions of capacity and religion. The Hanafi school, based on the general verse 4:3, permits a free man to marry a slave-girl without the condition of inability to marry a free woman, and allows marrying a Kitabiyya slave-girl. This lesson examines the evidence and the Shafi'i objection that requires both inability and the slave-girl being Muslim.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
A free man may marry a slave-girl, even a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian), without any condition of inability to marry a free woman.
This is the explicit statement 'al-amah wa law kitabiyya' – even if she is a Kitabiyya.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i restricts: a free man can only marry a slave-girl if he cannot afford a free woman, and she must be Muslim.
3
Compare the proofs
Hanafi uses the general verse 4:3 'Marry what seems good to you of women' which includes all women. Shafi'i uses verse 4:25 which explicitly conditions marriage to slave-girls on inability to marry free believing women.
4
Analyze the Hanafi argument against the condition
Hanafis argue that verse 4:25 establishes permissibility when the condition of inability is present, but does not negate permissibility when the condition is absent. The general verse remains operative.
This is a classic Hanafi usul principle: the mention of a condition does not imply exclusivity.
5
Note the ruling regarding Kitabiyya
The general verse includes Kitabiyya women (People of the Book) as they are included in 'women'. The Shafi'i restriction to Muslims is not supported by the text.

The Hanafi position, based on the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maimunah while in ihram, is that such marriage is valid. Al-Shafi'i rejects this, citing another hadith 'la yankih al-muhrim' (the one in ihram should not marry). The Hanafi response is that the latter hadith is weak, and if authentic, it refers to intercourse, not the marriage contract. Second: the permissibility of a free man marrying a slave-girl, even a Kitabiyya one. Hanafis uphold general permissibility based on the verse 'Marry what seems good to you of women' (4:3), while Shafi'i restricts it to cases of inability to marry a free woman and requires the slave-girl to be Muslim. Hanafis argue the general verse is not restricted by the condition of inability; the condition (in verse 4:25) is for a specific situation and does not negate permissibility otherwise. Third: the prohibition of marrying a slave-girl while already having a free wife (collecting them). Hanafis absolutely prohibit this, citing the hadith 'la tankih al-amah 'ala al-hurrah'. Shafi'i allows it for a slave, and Malik allows it with the free wife's consent. The Hanafi evidence is from hadith. An edge case is when the free wife is in her 'iddah: Abu Hanifah maintains the prohibition, while his two companions permit it if the 'iddah is from a ba'in divorce, because the free wife's marital bond is completely severed (they are 'separate'). Finally, the passage touches on the maximum number of wives: four for free women, with consensus that no more than four are allowed (refuting the view of al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim who allowed nine).

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave. The ruling on marrying her depends on her status and the husband's ability to marry a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the Hanafi school uses to permit a free man to marry a Kitabiyya slave-girl?
A) Verse 4:25
B) Verse 4:3
C) The practice of the companions
D) Analogy with free Kitabiyya women

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the general Quranic command to permit marriage to slave-girls unconditionally, including Kitabiyya.

Prohibition of Collecting a Slave-Girl with a Free Wife
Prohibition of collecting a slave-girl with a free wife; intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah; limit of four wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi prohibition of marrying a slave-girl while having a free wife, including the intra-madhhab difference on the 'iddah case.

Core Idea
The Prophet forbade marrying a slave-girl on top of a free woman; this prohibition is absolute, even during the free wife's 'iddah.

While a free man may marry a slave-girl if he has no wife, he cannot combine her with a free wife. The Hanafi school, based on a clear prophetic prohibition, makes this haram. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute when the free wife is in her 'iddah period: Abu Hanifah maintains the prohibition, but his two companions permit it if the 'iddah is ba'in (irrevocable). This lesson also covers the limit of four wives.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
It is forbidden (haram) to marry a slave-girl while already married to a free woman. This is based on the hadith 'Do not marry a slave-girl on top of a free woman.'
The prohibition applies to both the free man and, by analogy, to the free woman's rights.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah
Abu Hanifah says the prohibition applies even if the free wife is in her 'iddah (whether ba'in or raj'i). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is permissible if the 'iddah is ba'in because the marriage bond is severed.
The disagreement hinges on whether the free wife's rights are still intact during 'iddah.
3
Examine the proof for Abu Hanifah's view
Abu Hanifah analogizes to the case of marrying a sister during the 'iddah of another sister: the marital bond is still considered to exist in some respects.
4
Identify the limit on number of wives
The verse 4:3 limits to four free women. The Hanafi school and majority hold that no more than four wives are allowed; a minority view (al-Qasim) is rejected.
For slave-girls, the Hanafi view is that one may own as many as he wishes, but marrying more than one slave-girl? The passage says 'four from free women'.
5
Consolidate the thamarah
If a man marries a slave-girl while having a free wife, the marriage to the slave-girl is invalid (batil) according to the majority view. The dispute on 'iddah affects when the prohibition starts/ends.

(ط) لا عكسه: [ أي لا يحل نكاح الأمة على الحرة. (ع)] وهو تزوج الأمة على الحرة؛ لقوله عليه: "لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة"، وقال الشافعي: يجوز ذلك للعبد، وقال مالك: يجوز برضاء الحرة، والحجة عليهما ما روينا رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۷/۱] ولو في عدة الحرة: [سواء كانت عدة طلاق بائن أو رجعي أي لا يجوز تزوج الأمة على الحرة وإن كان في عدة الحرة. And the woman in ihram even if she is in the state of ihram: i.e., it is permissible to marry a woman in ihram even if the husband or the arranging guardian is in ihram. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The muhrim (one in ihram) should not marry and should not be married,' and in another version: 'should not propose' narrated by Muslim. Our proof: the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that he (peace be upon him) married Maimunah while he was in ihram, narrated by Muslim and others. And what he (Shafi'i) narrated is weak, according to al-Bukhari. And even if it is authentic, it is interpreted as referring to intercourse, because that is the literal meaning: i.e., the muhrim should not have intercourse and the muhrima should not allow intercourse. The masculine form (la yankih) is considering the person. If you say: Yazid ibn al-Asamm narrated that he married her while he was halal, I say: his narration does not contradict the narration of Ibn 'Abbas, and it is for this reason that 'Amr ibn Dinar al-Zuhri said: 'Does Ibn al-Asamm know? He is a Bedouin who urinates on his leg, do you make him like Ibn 'Abbas?' Or it is interpreted as meaning that by 'marriage' he intended the wedding night (bina') metaphorically, because it is its cause, so it is permissible to apply it to the wedding night. And His statement: 'and the slave-girl even if she is Kitabiyya' – this is according to us (Hanafis). Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible for a free man to marry a Kitabiyya slave-girl, subject to the condition of inability to marry a free woman, and [if he does] he marries a Muslim [slave-girl] due to the verse: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then [let him marry] from what your right hands possess' (al-Nisa' 4:25); he permitted marriage to slave-girls with two conditions: lack of ability and that she be believing. Our proof is the verse: 'Marry what seems good to you of women' (al-Nisa' 4:3) which is general and includes both slave-girls and free women. What follows establishes the ruling when the described condition is present, when the condition of inability exists, but does not negate or affirm its absence. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:197) And the slave-girl: i.e., it is also permissible to marry the slave-girl of another, even if he can afford [to marry] a free woman. (T) Not the opposite: i.e., it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl while having a free wife. (A) And that is marrying a slave-girl on top of a free wife, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Do not marry a slave-girl on top of a free woman.' (T, A)] It is connected to the previous: i.e., it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl on top of a free wife even if the free woman is in her 'iddah, according to Abu Hanifah. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is permissible if the 'iddah is from a ba'in divorce, because the prohibition is [due to] bringing the slave-girl into the same household as the free woman, and this [case] is separate. And the three [schools?] also said this. But he (Abu Hanifah) has: that the marriage [of the free woman] remains in some respects, like the marriage of a sister during the 'iddah of another sister. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:197) And four from among free women etc., due to the verse: 'Marry what seems good to you of women: two, three, or four' (al-Nisa' 4:3). Al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible from slave-girls except one, because its permissibility is out of necessity and that necessity is satisfied with one. And the proof against him is what we have recited. And His statement: 'only' indicates that it is not permissible to marry more than four, and there is consensus of the Ummah on that. Al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim said: it is permissible to marry nine...

Commentary
This final passage covers three distinct rulings with inter-madhhab disputes. First: the validity of marriage while in the state of ihram (hajj/umrah). The Hanafi position, based on the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maimunah while in ihram, is that such marriage is valid. Al-Shafi'i rejects this, citing another hadith 'la yankih al-muhrim' (the one in ihram should not marry). The Hanafi response is that the latter hadith is weak, and if authentic, it refers to intercourse, not the marriage contract. Second: the permissibility of a free man marrying a slave-girl, even a Kitabiyya one. Hanafis uphold general permissibility based on the verse 'Marry what seems good to you of women' (4:3), while Shafi'i restricts it to cases of inability to marry a free woman and requires the slave-girl to be Muslim. Hanafis argue the general verse is not restricted by the condition of inability; the condition (in verse 4:25) is for a specific situation and does not negate permissibility otherwise. Third: the prohibition of marrying a slave-girl while already having a free wife (collecting them). Hanafis absolutely prohibit this, citing the hadith 'la tankih al-amah 'ala al-hurrah'. Shafi'i allows it for a slave, and Malik allows it with the free wife's consent. The Hanafi evidence is from hadith. An edge case is when the free wife is in her 'iddah: Abu Hanifah maintains the prohibition, while his two companions permit it if the 'iddah is from a ba'in divorce, because the free wife's marital bond is completely severed (they are 'separate'). Finally, the passage touches on the maximum number of wives: four for free women, with consensus that no more than four are allowed (refuting the view of al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim who allowed nine).

Key Terms
لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة (la tankih al-amah 'ala al-hurrah): A prophetic hadith prohibiting the marriage of a slave-girl while having a free wife.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband.

Marriage During Ihram: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Hanafi ruling and proof for validity of marriage while in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that marriage contracted while in ihram is valid, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the primary dalil.

Core Idea
Marriage in ihram is valid in the Hanafi school — the Prophet's own practice overrides the general prohibition.

The state of ihram imposes several prohibitions, but does it prevent marriage? Both Hanafi and Shafi'i scholars differ on this point. This lesson unpacks the key hadith evidence and the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position, showing how the Hanafi school prioritizes the Prophet's actual practice over a verbal prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school holds regarding marriage while in ihram: it is valid and permissible regardless of whether the husband, wife, or guardian is muhrim.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that marriage during ihram is invalid. Note the hadith he cites: 'The muhrim should not marry nor be married.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: a hadith in Sahih Muslim. How do they use it to support their ruling?
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school offers a two-pronged rebuttal: first, the hadith is weak (per al-Bukhari); second, if authentic, it is interpreted as prohibiting intercourse, not the contract. Also note the strong counter-dalil from Ibn Abbas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a marriage contract during ihram is valid but intercourse is prohibited until after ihram. In the Shafi'i school, the contract itself is invalid and must be renewed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحرمة ولو محرماً : أي يحل تزوج المحرمة ولو كان الزوج أو الولي المزوج محرماً، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز؛ لقوله عليه: "لا ينكح المحرم ولا ينكح"، وفي رواية: "لا يخطب"، رواه مسلم ولنا: حديث ابن عباس ما " أنه عليه تزوج ميمونة وهو محرم"، رواه مسلم وغيره، وما رواه ضعيف قاله البخاري، ولئن صح فهو محمول على الوطء؛ لأنه الحقيقة أي لا يطأ المحرم ولا تمكن المحرمة من الوطء، والتذكير باعتبار الشخص، فإن قلت: روى يزيد بن الأصم أنه تزوج بها وهو حلال، قلت روايته لا تعارض رواية ابن عباس للها، ولهذا قال عمرو بن الدينار الزهري: وما يدري ابن الأصم أعرابي بوال على ساقه أتجعله مثل ابن عباس لما أو يحمل على أنه أراد بالتزوج البناء بها مجازاً؛ لأنه سببه، فجاز إطلاقه على البناء.
And the woman in ihram even if [the man is] in ihram: meaning it is permissible to marry a woman in ihram even if the husband or the marriage guardian is in ihram. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible, because of his (peace be upon him) saying: 'The muhrim should not marry nor be married', and in one narration: 'should not propose marriage', narrated by Muslim. And we have the hadith of Ibn Abbas that he (peace be upon him) married Maymunah while he was in ihram, narrated by Muslim and others. And what he narrated is weak, according to al-Bukhari. And even if it is authentic, it is interpreted as referring to sexual intercourse, because that is the literal meaning: that the muhrim should not have intercourse nor enable the muhrima to have intercourse. And the masculine form is by way of the person. If you say: Yazeed ibn al-Asamm narrated that he married her while he was halal, I say: his narration does not contradict the narration of Ibn Abbas; for this reason Amr ibn Dinar al-Zuhri said: 'What does Ibn al-Asamm know? A bedouin who urinates on his leg? Do you make him like Ibn Abbas?' Or it may be interpreted that by 'marriage' he meant intercourse metaphorically, because it is its cause, so it is permissible to apply it to intercourse.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of marriage while in the state of ihram. The Hanafi school holds that marriage contracted by a person in ihram (whether man or woman) is valid. The proof is the authentic hadith that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) married Maymunah while he was in ihram. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is invalid, relying on the hadith 'The muhrim should not marry nor be married' (Muslim). The Hanafi response is twofold: first, the hadith quoted by al-Shafi'i is weak according to al-Bukhari; second, even if accepted, the proper interpretation is that it prohibits sexual intercourse, not the marriage contract itself. The objection from Yazeed ibn al-Asamm's narration is dismissed on grounds of his lesser authority, or his statement is interpreted metaphorically. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that marrying while in ihram is valid.

Key Terms
مُحْرِم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah.
مُحْرِمَة (muhrima): A woman in the state of ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hadith that the Prophet married Maymunah while in ihram

D) Analogy to contracts during ihram

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates marriage in ihram, distinguishing between the contract (valid) and intercourse (prohibited), based on the Prophet's own practice.

Ruling on Marrying a Woman After Zina and the Case of al-Madmuma
Rulings on marriage after zina and a combined marriage with a prohibited woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a woman after zina and explain the mahr distribution in the case of al-madmuma, distinguishing the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A man may marry a woman he committed zina with, and in al-madmuma the entire mahr goes to the permissible wife per Abu Hanifa but is divided per the Sahibayn.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses two important nikah rulings. First, it clarifies that marrying a woman after committing zina with her is completely halal, not makruh or invalid. Second, it discusses the complex case of al-madmuma — when a man marries two women in one contract, one of whom is forbidden to him. The marriage of the permissible woman is valid, but the mahr distribution is disputed between Abu Hanifa and his two students. We will examine both views and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage gives two distinct rulings: (1) marriage after zina is permissible, (2) in a combined contract with a prohibited woman, the permissible marriage is valid and the prohibited one is void.
These rulings are independent but both appear in the text.
2
State the ruling on marriage after zina
The text says it is permissible (halal) to marry a woman after zina with her; the verse implying otherwise is said to be abrogated or interpreted differently.
The ruling is clear: the marriage contract is valid and consummation is allowed.
3
Define al-madmuma
Al-madmuma is the permissible woman who is joined in the marriage contract to a prohibited woman. The term highlights that she is 'joined' to a forbidden relative.
The valid marriage is the one to the permissible woman; the other is automatically invalid.
4
Analyze the mahr dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that the full specified mahr belongs to the permissible wife because the prohibited woman does not compete. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the mahr is consideration for both, so it must be divided proportionally based on mahr al-mithl.
Both views are recorded in the Hanafi school; no explicit tarjih is given here.
5
Apply the dispute to the example
The text gives an example: specified mahr = 1000, mahr al-mithl of prohibited = 2000, of permissible = 1000. According to the Sahibayn, the permissible receives 333.33 (1/3 of 1000). Abu Hanifa would give her the full 1000.
The calculation shows the Sahibayn's proportional method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أو زنا والمضمومة إلى محرّمة والمسمى لها، وبطل نكاح المتعة من المهر كله وعند الجمهور الآية منسوخة بقوله: ﴿وَأَنكِحُوا الْأَيَامَى ﴾ (النور: ۳۲) وبقوله: ﴿فَانْكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ (النساء:۳) وقيل: المراد بالنكاح الوطء، فالمعنى الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زانٍ في حالة الزنا، ثم اعلم أن الفراش ثلاثة أنواع: قوي ووسط وضعيف، فالقوي فراش المنكوحة يثبت نسب ولدها بلا إقرار، ولا ينتفي إلا باللعان، والوسط: فراش أم الولد يثبت النسب بلا إقرار، وينتفي بمجرد النفي والضعيف: فراش الأمة لا يثبت نسب ولدها بلا دعوة، ومعنى الفراش صيرورة المرأة بحال لو جاءت بولد ثبت نسبه من غير دعوة (مستخلص، فتح عيني) أو زنا: أي حل تزوج الموطوءة بالوطء زنا بأن رأى امرأة تزني فتزوجها جاز له أن يطأها. (ع) والمضمومة: أي حل تزوج المرأة المضمومة إلى امرأة محرمة بأن تزوج امرأتين أحدهما لا تحل له صح نكاح من تحل، وبطل نكاح الأخرى . (ع) والمسمى لها أي جميع المهر المسمى للمضمومة التي جاز نكاحها عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن المحرمة لا تزاحمها، وقالا يقسم على مهر مثلهما، فما أصاب المضمومة لزمه، وما أصاب المحرمة لا يلزمه حتى لو كان المسمى ألفاً ومهر مثل المحرمة ألفان، والمضمومة ألف كان للمضمومة ثلاث مائة مما وثلاثة وثلاثون وثلث عندهما؛ لأن المسمى مقابل لهما، فيكون منقسماً عليهما.
Section on or zina (marriage after fornication) and the woman joined to a prohibited woman and the mahr specified for her, and the invalidation of mut'ah marriage from the entire mahr. According to the majority, the verse is abrogated by His saying: 'Marry the unmarried' (24:32) and 'Marry what is lawful to you' (4:3). It is said the meaning of marriage is intercourse, so the meaning is: the fornicator does not marry except a fornicator in the state of fornication. Then know that the bed (firash) is of three types: strong, medium, and weak. The strong bed is the bed of the married wife; the lineage of her child is established without acknowledgment and is not negated except by li'an. The medium is the bed of the umm al-walad; lineage is established without acknowledgment and is negated merely by denial. The weak is the bed of the slave woman; the lineage of her child is not established without a claim. The meaning of bed (firash) is that the woman becomes in a state such that if she brings a child, its lineage is established without a claim. (Mukhtasar, Fath al-Qadir) 'Or zina' meaning: it is permissible to marry a woman who was penetrated by fornication, that if he sees a woman committing zina then marries her, it is permissible for him to have intercourse with her. (Inayah) 'And the woman joined to a prohibited woman' meaning: it is permissible to marry a woman who is joined to a prohibited woman, that if he marries two women one of whom is not lawful for him, the marriage of the one who is lawful is valid, and the marriage of the other is invalid. (Inayah) 'And the mahr specified for her' means all the specified mahr is for the joined woman whose marriage is valid according to Abu Hanifa, because the prohibited woman does not compete with her. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is divided according to the mahr of their like; whatever falls to the joined woman is binding, and whatever falls to the prohibited woman is not binding. So if the specified mahr is one thousand and the mahr of the like of the prohibited woman is two thousand, and that of the joined woman is one thousand, the joined woman gets three hundred and thirty-three and one-third according to them, because the specified mahr is in exchange for both, so it is divided between them.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage begins a section on marriage rulings. The text first states that a man may marry a woman with whom he committed zina; it is halal to consummate the marriage. Then it discusses the case of al-madmuma: if a man marries two women simultaneously, one of whom is forbidden (e.g., a close relative through suckling), the marriage of the permissible one is valid and the other is invalid. Regarding the mahr, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the full specified mahr goes to the permissible wife, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that the mahr is divided proportionally according to the mahr al-mithl of each woman. An example is given: if the specified mahr is 1000, and the mahr al-mithl of the forbidden woman is 2000 while that of the permissible is 1000, then the permissible woman receives 333.33. The basis of the Sahibayn's view is that the mahr is consideration for the marriage contract, which encompassed both women; since the contract for the forbidden woman is void, the mahr is apportioned. The passage also includes a brief digression on the three types of bed (firash) affecting lineage: the strong bed of a wife establishes lineage without acknowledgment, the medium of umm al-walad establishes lineage but can be negated by denial, and the weak of a slave woman requires a claim. This section is primarily a ruling statement with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
المضمومة (al-madmuma): A woman who is married while being joined (in the contract) to a woman who is prohibited to the husband
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary mahr of a woman similar to the bride in status

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how is the mahr divided when a man marries two women in one contract, one of whom is forbidden?
A) The mahr is divided equally between both women
B) The entire mahr goes to the permissible wife
C) The mahr is void entirely
D) The mahr is given to the forbidden wife's family

Key Takeaway
Marrying a woman after zina is halal, and in a combined contract, the mahr for the valid marriage may be subject to dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
