And a virgin adult (balighah) is not to be forced into marriage. If the guardian seeks her permission, and she keeps silent, or laughs, or cries, or he marries her and the news reaches her and she remains silent without being scornful, without sound — that is (the guardian acting) without seeking her permission after the marriage, i.e. the news of the marriage, i.e. the virgin adult. And know that guardianship is of two types: guardianship of recommendation (wilayat nadb), which is the guardianship over the sane adult, whether virgin or thayyib; and guardianship of compulsion (wilayat ijbar), which is the guardianship over the minor, whether virgin or thayyib, and likewise the adult insane and the slave. So the basis of compulsion with us is minority. Thus the meaning of his saying 'Do not force a virgin adult' is that it is not permissible for the guardian to force the virgin adult into marriage. Al-Shafi'i said: He has the right to force her, because the basis of compulsion with him is virginity, by analogy with the minor, since she is ignorant of the matter of marriage due to lack of experience. Our proof: she is free and legally responsible, so another cannot have authority over her. And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The virgin — her father seeks her permission,' narrated by Muslim. And guardianship over the minor is due to her deficient intellect, but here she has become complete by adulthood, as evidenced by the address of obligations to her and her right to dispose of her property. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath) ... So it is permission (idhn). And if someone other than the guardian seeks her permission, then speech is necessary, like the thayyib. (Then the commentary explains: ) So it is permission, i.e. each of the mentioned (silence, laughter, crying). As for silence, due to his ﷺ saying: 'Do not marry the thayyib until she is consulted, and do not marry the virgin until her permission is sought.' They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, how is her permission?' He said: 'By her silence,' narrated by Muslim. As for laughter, it is indicative consent because it is a sign of joy and happiness at what she heard, and likewise if she smiles. It is said: if she laughs scornfully at what she heard, it is not consent. Unlike if she cries, for that is a sign of displeasure and dislike. It is said: if she cries without sound, it is not rejection but sadness at leaving her family, and upon this is the fatwa. It is said: if her tears are cold, it is consent; if hot, it is not consent. In consultation, it is required to name the husband in a way that she recognizes him, so that her desire or aversion appears. Mention of the dowry is not required in the correct view. Then it is made equal between seeking permission and the arrival of the news of the marriage, because the indication in silence does not differ whether it is before or after the contract. Then, if the informer is a guardian, according to Abu Yusuf: her silence after the contract is rejection, and that is the statement of Muhammad. According to Abu Hanifa, it is consent. And the messenger of the guardian is like the guardian. If the informer is a non-relative (fuduli), then the conditions of number and uprightness (adalah) are required according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them (the two). (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 200/1) 'Other than the guardian': i.e. the distant guardian when the nearer is present. (T) By speech, and her silence is not consent, because her silence is due to lack of attention to their words, so it does not indicate consent. (T, A)
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage addresses two interrelated rulings: (1) a virgin adult cannot be coerced into marriage, and (2) the methods of consent for a virgin differ from those for a non-virgin. The text opens by stating that a virgin adult (bikr balighah) is not to be forced (la tujbir). It then distinguishes between two types of guardianship: wilayat nadb (guardianship of recommendation, i.e., over sane adults) and wilayat ijbar (guardianship of compulsion, i.e., over minors, insane, and slaves). The key principle: compulsion is based on minority, not virginity. Al-Shafi'i is mentioned as opposing this, arguing that a virgin can be forced due to her inexperience; the Hanafi response is that she is legally responsible (mukallafah), so no one can compel her. The hadith 'The virgin – her father seeks her permission' (Muslim) is cited to support this. The second part discusses how consent is indicated. If the guardian asks permission, the virgin's silence, laughter, or crying without sound indicate consent, while explicit speech is not required for the virgin. For the thayyib (non-virgin), explicit speech is necessary. The commentary elaborates on the nuances of each reaction, including the distinction between laughing scornfully (not consent) and crying silently (considered sadness, not rejection). The fatwa is that crying without sound is not rejection. It also discusses the scenario when the news of an already-conducted marriage reaches the woman: if the informer is the guardian, silence after the fact is considered consent according to Abu Hanifa, but rejection according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. If the informer is a non-relative (fuduli), strict conditions of number and uprightness are required per Abu Hanifa. Finally, if someone other than the guardian (e.g., a distant relative) seeks permission, then speech is required for consent, and silence is not sufficient because the woman may not pay attention to them. This passage is from the Kanz al-Daqa'iq commentary and falls under the authority of the mutakhkhirin, relying on the primary texts.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbar): Compulsion; a guardian's right to force a person into marriage against their will. In the Hanafi school, this applies only to minors and the insane.
بكر (bikr): A virgin; a woman who has never had sexual intercourse. In this context, a virgin whose hymen is intact.
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin; a woman who has had sexual intercourse. Her consent must be expressed explicitly in speech.
فضولي (fuduli): A non-relative informer; a person who is not the woman's guardian and reports the marriage to her. Strict conditions apply for her silence to be valid as consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa in the text, what is the ruling on a virgin who cries without sound when informed of her marriage?
A) It is rejection because crying shows displeasure.
B) It is consent because silent crying is sadness not rejection.
C) It depends on whether the tears are hot or cold.
D) It is null because crying indicates lack of consent.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's valid signs of consent are silence, laughter, and silent crying; silence only counts if the informer is her guardian, not a stranger.

Legal Virginity: Hanafi Ruling on Non-Sexual Hymen Break
Explaining that a woman with hymen broken by non-sexual causes is treated as a virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions regarding the legal status of a woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual means and apply the Hanafi ruling for consent in marriage.

Core Idea
A woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual means is legally a virgin: her silence suffices for marriage consent.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: how to classify a woman whose hymen is lost through non-sexual causes such as jumping, menstruation, or prolonged stay at home. The ruling has direct implications for marriage consent — whether her silence is enough or explicit speech is required. We will follow the Hanafi reasoning based on social custom (urf) and the legal definition of virginity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its scope
The Hanafi school holds that a woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual causes (or even by zina, according to Abu Hanifa) is legally a virgin for marriage consent. Her silence is considered consent.
This applies to causes like wathbah (jumping), hayd (menstruation), jarah (injury), and ta'nis (prolonged stay).
2
Identify the opposing position
Al-Shafi'i, and (regarding zina) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argue that since the hymen is broken, she is physically a thayyib and must explicitly consent.
The Shafi'i school also holds this for non-sexual causes.
3
Follow the opposing proof
The opposition relies on the physical fact of the broken hymen and the hadith that the thayyib must be consulted, which they interpret as requiring speech.
Hadith: 'The thayyib is consulted' and 'the thayyib's tongue expresses her'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the legal definition of virginity for consent is not purely physical; it considers social reality (urf) and modesty. The woman is a virgin in actuality (first deflowerer), so requiring speech would cause embarrassment.
Conflicting principles: linguistic definition vs. social custom.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In the Hanafi school, her silence suffices; in the Shafi'i school, she must verbally consent. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's position.
This affects how the marriage contract is conducted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالثيب : [وهي من زالت بكارتها أي لابد من نطقها. (ط،ع)] يشير إلى أنه لابد من نطقها واستدل عليه بقوله عليها: "الثيب تشاور"، لكن فيه ليس دلالة على اشتراط النطق، فإن البكر أيضاً تشاور، وكذا الرضا بالقول لا يشترط في حق الثيب أيضاً، بل رضاها يتحقق تارة بالقول كقولها: رضیت وقبلت ونحوهما، وتارة بالدلالة كطلب مهرها أو نفقتها أو تمكينها من الوطء ونحوها، فثبت بهذا أنه لا فرق بينهما في اشتراط الاستئذان والرضا، وأن رضاهما قد يكون صريحاً وقد يكون دلالة، غير أن سكوت البكر رضا دلالة لحيائها دون الثيب؛ لأن حيائها قل بالممارسة فلا يدل سكوتها على الرضا رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۱/۱] وقال في الفتح: إنه تظاهرت الدلالة على اختصاصها بالقول ففي حديث أبي هريرة: "لا تنكح الأيم حتى تستأمر " والأمر لا يكون بغير القول، وأصرح منه قوله في حديث آخر: "والثيب يعرب عنها لسانها". بوثبة: أي وثوب من فوق إلى أسفل أو تعنيس: هو إطالة مكثها في منزل أهلها بعد البلوغ. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۱/۱] فهي بكر: [حكماً حتى يكون أحكامها أحكام البكر في التزويج. (ع)] أي من زالت بكارتها بهذه الأشياء فهي في حكم الأبكار في أن سكوتها رضاً، وقال الشافعي: لا يكون حكمها كحكم الأبكار؛ لأن البكر اسم لامرأة عذرتها قائمة، والثيب خلافها، وهذه زالت عذرتها فكانت ثيباً، ولنا أنها بكر حقيقة في ما عدا الزنا؛ لأن مصيبها أول مصيب، وأما من زالت بكارتها بزنا فهي كذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا والشافعي في الجديد وأحمد في رواية: لا يكتفي بسكوتها؛ لأنها ثيب حقيقة؛ لأن مصيبها عائدة إليها، ولأبي حنيفة: أن الناس عرفوها بكراً فيعيبونها بالنطق .
Like the thayyib: [i.e. she whose virginity has been removed, thus she must speak. (T, A)] He indicates that she must speak, and he deduces it from his ﷺ saying: 'The thayyib is consulted,' but in that there is no indication of the condition of speech, for the virgin is also consulted. Likewise, consent by speech is not specifically required for the thayyib either; rather, her consent is realized sometimes by speech, such as her saying 'I am pleased' or 'I accept' and the like, and sometimes by indication, such as asking for her dowry, maintenance, or permitting intercourse, etc. So it is established that there is no difference between them in requiring consultation and consent, and that their consent may be explicit or by indication. However, the silence of the virgin is indicative consent due to her modesty, unlike the thayyib because her modesty is lessened by practice, so her silence does not indicate consent. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 201/1) Al-Fath says: The evidence has shown that the thayyib is specifically [required] to speak, because in the hadith of Abu Hurayrah: 'Do not marry the unmarried woman (ayyim) until she is consulted' and consultation cannot be without speech; and more explicit than this is his saying in another hadith: 'The thayyib's tongue expresses her.' By jumping (wathbah): i.e. jumping from above to below; or 'aniness (ta'nis): i.e. her prolonged stay in her family's home after puberty. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 201/1) She is a virgin: [legally, so that her rulings are the rulings of a virgin in marriage. (A)] i.e. she whose virginity has been removed by these things is in the ruling of virgins in that her silence is consent. Al-Shafi'i said: Her ruling is not like that of virgins, because 'virgin' is a name for a woman whose hymen is intact, and 'thayyib' is the opposite. This woman's hymen has been removed, so she is a thayyib. Our proof: She is a virgin in reality except in the case of zina, because the one who deflowers her is the first one to deflower her. As for she whose virginity is removed by zina, she is [treated] similarly according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i in the new position, and Ahmad in one narration, hold that her silence is not sufficient, because she is a real thayyib, since the deflowerer (musib) returns to her. According to Abu Hanifa, people know her as a virgin, so it would be embarrassing for her to speak.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage deals with two sub-issues: (1) the definition of a thayyib and the requirement of explicit speech for her consent, and (2) the legal status of a woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual causes such as jumping (wathbah), menstruation (hayd), injury (jarah), prolonged stay at home (ta'nis), or even zina (unlawful intercourse). The text begins by discussing the thayyib. It clarifies that the hadith 'the thayyib is consulted' does not necessarily mean she must speak; consent can be by indication too. However, due to her reduced modesty, silence is not considered consent for a thayyib; she must express herself either verbally or by clear actions (like asking for mahr). The second part addresses the woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual means. The Matn states: 'She who loses her virginity by jumping or menstruation etc. is a virgin [in legal rulings].' The commentary adds that such a woman is treated as a virgin in marriage: her silence is consent. This is the Hanafi position. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that since her hymen is broken, she is a thayyib in reality and thus must speak. The Hanafi rebuttal is that she is a virgin in reality (because the one who deflowers her is the first one; in non-sexual causes, there is no deflowerer). If her virginity is lost by zina, Abu Hanifa still treats her as a virgin for consent purposes, based on social custom: people consider her a virgin, and requiring her to speak would be embarrassing. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad consider her a real thayyib and require explicit speech. This passage highlights the Hanafi school's consideration of social context (urf) in legal rulings. The authority weight is from the later commentaries (Ramz al-Haqa'iq, Fath) but based on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
بكر حكماً (bikr ḥukman): A woman who is legally considered a virgin for marriage consent rulings, even though her hymen is physically broken by non-sexual means.
بوثبة (bi-wathbah): By jumping; a specific cause of hymen breakage from a fall or jump from a height.
تعنيس (ta'nis): Prolonged stay in her family's home after puberty, causing the hymen to deteriorate naturally.
أيم (ayyim): A woman who has no husband; can refer to both virgin and thayyib. In the hadith, it is used for the thayyib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is considered a thayyib and must speak for consent
B) She is considered a virgin and her silence is consent
C) She is not allowed to marry until her consent is obtained in writing
D) Her sexual history must be investigated

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a woman whose hymen is broken by non-sexual means (or even zina per Abu Hanifa) is legally a virgin; her silence is consent for marriage.

Modes of Consent in Marriage: Silence, Laughter, Crying
Ruling on modes of consent in marriage with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consent for a virgin and a thayyib, and apply the rules regarding silence, laughter, and crying as indicators of consent.

Core Idea
Silence of a virgin is consent; a thayyib must speak or act clearly.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies how a woman's consent can be expressed in marriage. The Prophet's hadith establishes that a virgin's silence suffices, while a thayyib must be consulted. But what about laughter or tears? And what if she is informed after the contract? This lesson unpacks the evidence and the intra-school disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving consent (idhn) for marriage. The ruling is that the marriage is valid (sahih) when consent is properly expressed. For a virgin, silence suffices; for a thayyib, explicit speech or clear indication is required.
Consent is a condition (shart) for the contract.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Do not marry the thayyib until she is consulted, and do not marry the virgin until she is asked permission... She is silent.' (Muslim). This shows that a virgin's silence is her permission.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's silence for the virgin indicates that silence is a sufficient expression of consent due to her shyness. For the thayyib, the absence of such a ruling implies speech is needed.
Laughter indicates joy and is consent by indication; crying usually indicates dislike, but silent tears may be sadness, not rejection.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
After the contract, if the guardian informs her and she remains silent: Abu Hanifa says it is consent; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is rejection. Cross-madhhab: Shafi'i requires explicit speech for both if they are of age.
The fatwa follows the distinction between virgin and thayyib.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The hadith is the basis, and the majority of the school adopts the distinction: silence of virgin is consent; thayyib must speak or indicate clearly. The fatwa is on that.
See also the mention in al-Fath that evidence restricts the thayyib to speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
باب الأولياء والأكفاء

فهو إذن، وإن استأذلها غير الولي فلا بد من القول كاليب، ومن زالت بكارتها بوثبة أو حيضة أو جراحة أو تعنيس أو زنا فهي بكر، أصابت موضع البكارة أي بسبب زنا غير مشهور

فهو إذن [ أي كل واحد مما ذكر من السكوت والضحك والبكاء.] أما السكوت؛ فلقوله عليه: "لا تنكح الثيب حتى تستأمر، ولا تنكح البكر حتى تستأذن، قالوا: وكيف إذنها يا رسول الله؟ قال: "تسكت"، رواه مسلم، وأما الضحك فرضاء دلالة؛ لأنه علامة السرور والفرح بما سمعت وكذا إذا تبسمت، وقيل: إذا ضحكت كالمستهزئة بما سمعت لا يكون رضاً، بخلاف ما إذا بكت، فإنه دليل السخط والكراهة، وقيل: إذا بكت بلا صوت لم يكن رداً، بل حزناً على مفارقة أهلها، وعليه الفتوى، وقيل: إن كان دمعها بارداً يكون رضا، وإن كان حاراً لا يكون رضا، ويعتبر في الاستيمار تسمية الزوج على وجه يقع لها به المعرفة؛ لتظهر رغبتها فيه أو إعراضها عنه، ولا يشترط ذكر المهر في الصحيح ثم إنه سوي بين الاستئذان وبين بلوغ الخبر بالتزويج؛ لأن وجه الدلالة في السكوت لا يختلف بين أن يكون قبل العقد أو بعده، ثم المخبر إن كان ولياً فعن أبي يوسف: أن سكوتها بعد العقد يكون ردا وهو قول محمد وعند أبي حنيفة يكون رضا ورسول الولى كالولي، ولو كان المخبر فضولياً . يشترط فيه العدد والعدالة عند أبي حنيفة، خلافاً لهما. غير الولي: أي الولي الأبعد مع وجود الأقرب. من القول ولا يكون سكوتها رضا؛ لأن سكوتها لقلة الالتفات إلى كلامهم فلا يدل على الرضا. كالثيب : [وهي من زالت بكارتها أي لابد من نطقها.] يشير إلى أنه لابد من نطقها واستدل عليه بقوله عليها: "الثيب تشاور"، لكن فيه ليس دلالة على اشتراط النطق، فإن البكر أيضاً تشاور، وكذا الرضا بالقول لا يشترط في حق الثيب أيضاً، بل رضاها يتحقق تارة بالقول كقولها: رضیت وقبلت ونحوهما، وتارة بالدلالة كطلب مهرها أو نفقتها أو تمكينها من الوطء ونحوها، فثبت بهذا أنه لا فرق بينهما في اشتراط الاستئذان والرضا، وأن رضاهما قد يكون صريحاً وقد يكون دلالة، غير أن سكوت البكر رضا دلالة لحيائها دون الثيب؛ لأن حيائها قل بالممارسة فلا يدل سكوتها على الرضا وقال في الفتح: إنه تظاهرت الدلالة على اختصاصها بالقول ففي حديث أبي هريرة: "لا تنكح الأيم حتى تستأمر " والأمر لا يكون بغير القول، وأصرح منه قوله في حديث آخر: "والثيب يعرب عنها لسانها".
Book of Marriage. Chapter of Guardians and Equivalents. So it is permission. If someone other than the guardian seeks her permission, then speech is necessary, like the thayyib. And she whose hymen is lost by a jump, menstruation, a wound, prolonged spinsterhood, or fornication is a virgin—her hymen was affected, i.e., due to unpublicized fornication. So it is permission [i.e., each of what was mentioned of silence, laughter, and crying]. As for silence: because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not marry the thayyib until she is consulted, and do not marry the virgin until she is asked permission.' They said: 'How is her permission, O Messenger of God?' He said: 'She is silent.' Narrated by Muslim. As for laughter: it is consent by indication because it is a sign of joy and happiness with what she heard. Likewise if she smiles. And it is said: if she laughs as if mocking what she heard, it is not consent. Contrary to if she cries, for it is a sign of displeasure and dislike. And it is said: If she cries without a sound, it is not rejection but sadness at leaving her family. On that is the fatwa. And it is said: If her tears are cold, it is consent; if hot, it is not consent. In consultation, mentioning the husband is required in a way that she knows him, so that her desire for him or aversion can appear. Mentioning the dowry is not a condition in the correct view. Then he equated seeking permission before the contract and being informed of the marriage after the contract, because the basis of indication in silence does not differ between before or after the contract. Then if the informant is the guardian: from Abu Yusuf that her silence after the contract is rejection—that is the statement of Muhammad. And with Abu Hanifa, it is consent. And the messenger of the guardian is like the guardian. And if the informant is a non-guardian (fuduli), Abu Hanifa requires number and uprightness, contrary to the two. Non-guardian: i.e., the more distant guardian when the nearer exists. By speech her silence is not consent because her silence is due to inattention to their speech, so it does not indicate consent. Like the thayyib: [i.e., she whose hymen is lost—she must speak.] It indicates that she must speak, and it is supported by his statement (peace be upon him): 'The thayyib is consulted.' But there is no indication in it of requiring speech, for the virgin is also consulted. Likewise, consent by speech is not required in the case of the thayyib; rather, her consent is sometimes realized by speech—like saying 'I am satisfied' or 'I accept' and similar—and sometimes by indication—like asking for her dowry, her maintenance, or allowing intercourse, and similar. So it is established by this that there is no difference between them in requiring consultation and consent, and that their consent may be explicit or by indication. However, the silence of the virgin is consent by indication due to her shyness, unlike the thayyib because her shyness is lessened by practice, so her silence does not indicate consent. And it is said in al-Fath: The evidence has mounted for her being restricted to speech, as in the hadith of Abu Hurairah: 'Do not marry the spinster (ayyim) until she is consulted,' and consultation cannot be without speech. And more explicit is his statement in another hadith: 'The thayyib is expressed by her tongue.'
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the modes of consent in marriage. The author states that silence, laughter, and crying constitute permission (idhn) from a woman. He provides the hadith from Muslim where the Prophet (peace be upon him) states that a virgin's permission is her silence, while a thayyib must be consulted (musta'mar). Laughter is a sign of joy and thus indicates consent, but if it appears mocking, it is not. Crying indicates displeasure, but silent tears are not necessarily rejection; they may be sadness. The fatwa is that if she cries without sound, it is not a rejection. The author then discusses the condition of naming the husband when seeking permission. After the contract, if she is informed by the guardian, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says silence is consent, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is rejection. If a non-guardian informs her, she must explicitly speak because her silence might be due to inattention. Finally, the author clarifies that both a virgin and a thayyib may consent explicitly or by indication, but the difference is that a virgin's silence is consent by indication due to her shyness, whereas a thayyib's silence is not, because she is experienced.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukūt): Silence of the woman after being asked or informed about a marriage proposal, which is deemed as consent for a virgin.
رضا (riḍā): Consent of a woman required for a valid marriage contract; can be explicit (by speech) or implicit (by indication such as silence, laughter, etc.).
بكر (bikr): A woman who has never had sexual intercourse; her hymen is intact. For legal consent, she is the one whose silence suffices as consent.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has had lawful sexual intercourse; her consent must be expressed verbally or through clear actions, not silence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the consent of a virgin (bikr) when a marriage proposal is presented to her?
A) She must explicitly say 'I accept' for the marriage to be valid.
B) Her silence is considered consent due to her natural shyness.
C) She must speak aloud three times.
D) Her guardians must consent on her behalf.

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence is consent by indication due to shyness; a thayyib must express consent through speech or a clear action.

Definition of Virgin and Non-Virgin for Consent
Ruling on women whose hymen is lost – virgin or thayyib for consent?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats a woman whose hymen is lost by accidental causes or hidden zina as a virgin for marriage consent, and contrast this with the Shafi‘i position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a woman's legal status for consent depends on public perception, not just physical virginity.

This passage addresses a critical distinction in marriage law: when does a woman's silence suffice as consent? Physical virginity is one factor, but the Hanafi school looks deeper — to the cause of hymen loss and how the woman is perceived in society. Understanding this helps you see how fiqh balances legal rules with social realities.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: which women are considered 'virgins in ruling' (bikr ḥukman) and what legal effect this has (silence = consent).
The text lists causes: jump, ta‘nīs, menstruation, wound, and hidden zina according to Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Al-Shafi‘i holds that any hymen loss makes a woman a thayyib. Understand his proof: the hymen is the defining criterion.
Also note the disagreement within Hanafi: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on zina.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that the cause matters: if the first penetration was not by lawful marriage, she is still a virgin in people's eyes. Her silence is sufficient to avoid harm and embarrassment.
This is a talīl (reasoning) based on custom (‘urf) and public perception.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in a Hanafi court, such a woman can be married with silence; in a Shafi‘i court, she must speak.
The same physical woman gets different rulings based on the madhhab.
5
Note the exceptions
Women whose zina becomes widely known, or who were penetrated by shubhah or invalid marriage, are thayyib by consensus.
These exceptions show that the Hanafi ruling is not absolute; it is a nuanced balance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زالت بكارتها بوثبة: أي وثوب من فوق إلى أسفل أو تعنيس: هو إطالة مكثها في منزل أهلها بعد البلوغ. فهي بكر: [حكماً حتى يكون أحكامها أحكام البكر في التزويج.] أي من زالت بكارتها بهذه الأشياء فهي في حكم الأبكار في أن سكوتها رضاً، وقال الشافعي: لا يكون حكمها كحكم الأبكار؛ لأن البكر اسم لامرأة عذرتها قائمة، والثيب خلافها، وهذه زالت عذرتها فكانت ثيباً، ولنا أنها بكر حقيقة في ما عدا الزنا؛ لأن مصيبها أول مصيب، وأما من زالت بكارتها بزنا فهي كذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا والشافعي في الجديد وأحمد في رواية: لا يكتفي بسكوتها؛ لأنها ثيب حقيقة؛ لأن مصيبها عائدة إليها، ولأبي حنيفة: أن الناس عرفوها بكراً فيعيبونها بالنطق . فيكتفي بسكوتها كيلا يتعطل عليها مصالحها؛ وهذا إذا كان زناها خفياً حتى لو اشتهر حالها لا يكتفي بسكوتها بلا خلاف، وكذا لو وطئت بشبهة أو بنكاح فاسد تكون في حكم الثيب بلا خلاف.
And she whose hymen is lost by a jump (wathbah): i.e., jumping from above downwards; or 'ta‘nīs': i.e., prolonging her stay in her family's home after puberty. She is a virgin in ruling, so that her rulings are the rulings of a virgin in marriage. That is, she whose hymen is lost by these things is in the ruling of virgins in that her silence is consent. Al-Shafi‘i said: Her ruling is not like the ruling of virgins because a virgin is a woman whose hymen is intact, and a thayyib is the opposite, and this woman's hymen is lost, so she is a thayyib. Our evidence: She is a virgin in reality except regarding zina, because her first encounter is the first encounter. As for she whose hymen is lost by zina, she is also (considered a virgin) according to Abu Hanifa. The two disciples, al-Shafi‘i in the new view, and Ahmad in one narration said: Her silence is not sufficient because she is a thayyib in reality, because her encounter (zina) returns upon her. Abu Hanifa's argument: People know her as a virgin, so they would blame her for speaking, so her silence suffices so that her interests are not obstructed. This is if her zina was hidden; if her condition became widely known, her silence does not suffice without disagreement. Likewise, if she was penetrated by a dubious act or by an invalid marriage, she is in the ruling of a thayyib without disagreement.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal definition of a virgin (bikr) for the purpose of consent in marriage. The text first lists causes that remove the hymen: a jump from above, menstruation, a wound, prolonged unmarried status after puberty (ta‘nīs), and zina. It rules that a woman who loses her hymen by any of these, except possibly zina according to the disciples, is still considered a virgin in the sense that her silence suffices as consent. The Shafi‘i school disagrees, arguing that the hymen is the defining factor; once it is broken, she is a thayyib. The Hanafi reasoning is that the cause of loss matters: if it is not by lawful intercourse, she is still psychologically a virgin, and people consider her so. For zina, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says if the zina was hidden, she is still treated as a virgin to avoid harm, while the two disciples and others say she is a thayyib. The text also clarifies that if the zina was widely known, or if the penetration was by dubious means (shubhah) or invalid marriage, all agree she is a thayyib.

Key Terms
بكر حكمًا (bikr ḥukman): A woman who is physically a non-virgin due to accidental causes but legally treated as a virgin for the purpose of marriage consent.
تعنيس (ta‘nīs): Prolonged stay in the father's house after puberty without marriage; considered a cause that may break the hymen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on a woman whose hymen is lost due to hidden fornication (zina) regarding her silence as consent?
A) She is considered a thayyib and must speak.
B) She is considered a virgin and her silence suffices.
C) Her consent is invalid because of the zina.
D) Only her father can give consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi marriage law, a woman's legal status for consent hinges on the cause of hymen loss and public perception, not physical fact alone.

Whose Word is Taken in a Marriage Consent Dispute?
Ruling on dispute between woman and wali about consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is believed in a dispute over marriage consent and explain the intra-madhhab difference regarding the oath.

Core Idea
The woman's word is accepted because she denies a new occurrence, but an oath may be required.

When a woman and her guardian disagree about whether she consented to her marriage, the court must decide whose claim to accept. This lesson examines the Hanafi position and an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding an oath. You will learn the legal reasoning and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and parties
The dispute is between the woman (who denies consent) and her wali (who claims she consented by silence). Determine who is the claimant and who is the defendant.
Silence is ambiguous; it may indicate consent or refusal.
2
State the main Hanafi position
The woman's word is accepted because she denies the contract, which is a new occurrence; the default is no contract. The wali claims a binding right, so he must prove it.
3
Contrast Zafar's opposing view
Zafar argues that the wali's word should be accepted because silence is the original state, and refusal is a change that needs proof. Understand this reasoning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zafar
The Hanafi response: silence is not a default; it is ambiguous. The wali claims a newly arisen right (ownership of her sexual organ), so he is the claimant. The woman denies, so she holds the default of no contract.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on oath and the mufta bihi
Abu Hanifa says no oath is needed. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require an oath. The fatwa is on the disciples' view, so the woman must swear that she did not consent.
This affects courtroom procedure: the woman must take an oath before her word is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول لها [ أي للمرأة بيمينها على المفتى به (ط)] وقال زفر: القول قوله؛ لأن السكوت أصل والرد شاهداً له، ولنا أنه يدعي عليها لزوم العقد وملك بضعها وهو أمر حادث، وهي تنكر حدوثه، فكانت هي المستمسكة بالأصل فيكون القول قولها، ثم أيهما أقام البينة قبلت بينته، وإن أقاماها معاً فالبينة بينة المرأة، وإن لم يكن لهما بينة فالقول قولها بلا يمين عند أبي حنيفة وقالا عليها اليمين والفتوى على قولهما.
And the saying is hers [i.e., for the woman with her oath according to the muftā bihī]. Zafar said: The saying is his (the wali's) because silence is the basis and refusal is a witness for him. Our evidence: He claims binding of the contract and ownership of her sexual organ—a new occurrence—and she denies it, so she is the one adhering to the original state, thus the saying is hers. Then whichever of them presents evidence, his evidence is accepted. If both present evidence together, the evidence of the woman is preferred. If neither has evidence, then the saying is hers without oath according to Abu Hanifa, while the two said she must take an oath, and the fatwa is on their saying.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about whose statement is accepted when the woman and her guardian (wali) differ over whether she consented to the marriage. The text states that the woman's word is taken, supported by a statement that she is the one who denies the occurrence of the contract, and thus she is the one adhering to the original state (no marriage). Zafar differs, arguing that the guardian's claim is supported by the apparent silence of the woman. The text then discusses evidence: if either party brings witnesses, those witnesses are accepted; if both bring witnesses, the woman's evidence is preferred because she denies. If no evidence, the woman's word is taken. There is a further dispute: Abu Hanifa says no oath is needed, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say she must swear an oath. The fatwa is according to the two disciples.

Key Terms
القول لها (al-qawlu lahā): The legal maxim that the woman's claim is accepted in a dispute about consent, as she is the one denying the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (muftā bihī) in the Hanafi school, what is required of a woman who claims she did not consent when her wali says she did?
A) She must produce two male witnesses.
B) Her word is accepted without any condition.
C) She must take an oath that she did not consent.
D) The wali’s word is accepted because he is the guardian.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over marriage consent, the woman's denial is accepted because she denies a new occurrence, and according to the relied-upon fatwa, she must take an oath.

Wali's Authority to Marry Minors: A Three-School Dispute
Ruling on which guardians can marry minors coercively

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which guardians can marry minors coercively and compare it with the Maliki and Shafi'i positions, identifying the key proofs and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Any agnate guardian may marry a minor; the minor can annul at puberty – this safeguards interest.

Who can marry off a minor? The Hanafi school gives broad authority to all agnate guardians, while Malik and Shafi‘i restrict it. This lesson unpacks the dispute, showing how each school uses text, analogy, and consideration of the minor's welfare. The Hanafi position is reinforced by the minor's right to annul the marriage upon attaining puberty.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: any agnate guardian ('asabah) may coercively marry a minor boy or girl. The text says 'وللولي' (to the guardian) generally, covering all agnates.
The guardian must be a free, sane adult, but even a wrongdoer (fasiq) may act.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Malik holds only the father for a minor girl. Shafi‘i holds the father and grandfather for minors. Understand why each restricts the circle.
Malik cites lack of desire; Shafi‘i fears insufficient compassion.
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
Malik uses a hadith: 'The virgin is married by her father' (البكر يزوجها أبوها) and the example of Abu Bakr marrying ‘A’ishah. Shafi‘i uses an analogy (qiyas): if a non-father/grandfather cannot manage the minor's property, how can he manage the more important matter of marriage?
Shafi‘i's argument is a fortiori (اولى).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school replies: (1) The need to secure a compatible match exists for minors; (2) the minor has the option to annul (khiyar al-faskh) at puberty, which remedies any defect – unlike property transactions which cannot be undone. Also, the companions and general acceptance support the broader view.
The Hanafi response uses qiyas but differently: marriage benefits are secured by the guardian, and annulment is a safeguard.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, a minor can be married by any agnate; in Maliki only by father; in Shafi‘i by father or grandfather. This affects the validity of marriages contracted by uncles, brothers, etc.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the expansive view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللولي إلخ [ أي يجوز للولي عدلا كان أو فاسقا أباً كان أو غيره من الأولياء. (ط)] أطلق الولي إلخ: ليتناول الأب والجد وغيرهما من العصبات أي يجوز للولي خاصة أن ينكح الصغير والصغيرة جبراً، وهذا عندنا؛ لأن ولاية الإجبار عليهما لصغرهما، وقال مالك: لا يجوز لغير الأب؛ لأن ولاية الإجبار عنده مخصوصة بالأب وحده على الصغيرة وحدها دون الصغير، ويقول: إن الولاية على الحرة إنما ثبتت باعتبار الحاجة ولا حاجة لصغيرة؛ لانعدام الشهوة إلا أن ولاية الأب ثبتت نصاً وهو قوله عليها "البكر يزوجها أبوها"، أن أبا بكر له زوج عائشة النبي ، والجد ليس في معناه، فلا يلحق به، قلنا: بل هو موافق القياس؛ لأن النكاح ينتظم المصالح، ولا يتفق الكفؤ في كل زمان فأثبتنا الولاية له في حالة الصغر إحرازاً للكفؤ. وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز تزويجها لغير الأب والجد؛ لأن النظر لا يتم بالتفويض إلى غيرهما؛ لقصور الشفقة لبعد قرابة غيرهما ، ألا ترى أن غيره هما لا يملك التصرف في مال الصغير مع أن المال أدنى رتبة فكيف يملك التصرف في النفس مع كونه أعلى رتبة ولنا أن القرابة داعية إلى النظر كما في الأب والجد وما فيه من القصور أظهرناه بأن للصغير والصغيرة خيار الفسخ بخلاف التصرف في المال لأنه يكرر بتداول الأيدي، فلا يمكن تدارك الخلل بعد تداول الأيدي، فلا يثبت ولاية التصرف لغير الأب والجد في مال الصغير، وأما النكاح فيقع مرة ولا يتكرر، فلو وقع فيه خلل يمكن تداركه بالإعراض بعد البلوغ، فيثبت الولاية فيه لغير الأب والجد، ومذهبنا منقول عن عمر وعلي والعبادلة وأبي هريرة الله وكفا بهم حجة وقدوة، وأيضاً أجمع العلماء على العمل بقوله عليه: "الإنكاح إلى العصبات في حق الكبيرة"، فكذا في حق الصغيرة؛ لأنها أعجز وأمس حاجة.
And to the guardian etc. [i.e., it is permissible for the guardian, whether upright or immoral, whether father or others of the guardians.] He made 'guardian' general to include the father, grandfather, and other 'asabāt (agnates), i.e., it is permissible specifically for the guardian to marry the minor boy and minor girl coercively. This is our view because the authority of coercion upon them is due to their minority. Malik said: It is not permissible for other than the father because the authority of coercion in his view is confined to the father alone for the minor girl only, not the minor boy. He says: Authority over a free woman is established by consideration of need, and a minor girl has no need because she lacks desire; however, the father's authority is established by text—his statement (peace be upon him): 'The virgin is married by her father'—and Abu Bakr married 'A'ishah to the Prophet, and the grandfather is not in his meaning, so he is not annexed to him. We say: Rather, it conforms to analogy because marriage arranges benefits, and a compatible match is not found at all times, so we established authority for him in the state of minority to secure the compatible match. Al-Shafi‘i said: It is not permissible to marry her to other than the father and grandfather because proper consideration cannot be achieved by delegating to others due to the insufficiency of compassion due to the distance of others. Do you not see that other than them cannot dispose of the minor's property even though property is of lower rank, so how can he dispose of the person while it is of higher rank? Our evidence: Kinship calls for consideration as in the father and grandfather, and any deficiency appears because the minor boy and girl have the option of annulment, unlike disposal of property because it is repeatedly transferred, so a defect cannot be remedied after transfer of possession. Therefore, the authority of disposal for other than the father and grandfather is not established over the minor's property. As for marriage, it occurs once and is not repeated, so if a defect occurs, it can be remedied by annulment after puberty; hence, authority in it is established for other than the father and grandfather. Our school is transmitted from 'Umar, 'Ali, the 'Abādilah (Ibn 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, etc.), and Abu Hurairah—sufficient as proof and model. Also, the scholars agreed to act on his statement (peace be upon him): 'Marriage is for the agnates regarding the adult woman,' so similarly regarding the minor girl because she is more helpless and in greater need.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute on the authority of guardians to contract marriage for minors. The Hanafi school states that any guardian (wali) from the 'asabah (agnates) may marry off a minor boy or girl coercively (jabran). Malik holds that only the father can do so for a minor girl, citing that the mother of believers 'A’ishah was married by her father. Shafi‘i restricts it to the father and grandfather, arguing that others lack sufficient compassion. The Hanafi response uses analogy (qiyas): a minor needs a compatible spouse, which may not be available later; hence, the guardian is authorized. Moreover, the minor has the right of option (khiyar al-faskh) at puberty, which protects against potential abuse, unlike property transactions which are final. The Hanafi position is supported by the practice of the Companions and the general principle that marriage is for the agnates. The passage also notes the order of guardianship follows inheritance (first son, then father, etc.).

Key Terms
ولي (walī): A guardian who has the authority to contract marriage, specifically the agnate relatives (asabah) in order of inheritance, including father, grandfather, son, brother, etc.
جبر (jabr): Coercive authority of the guardian to marry a minor without the minor's consent due to his minority and need.
خيار الفسخ (khiyār al-faskh): The option of annulment that a minor boy or girl has upon reaching puberty if they were married off as minors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who among the following can validly contract a marriage for a minor boy or girl?
A) Only the father.
B) Only the father and grandfather.
C) Any agnate guardian (asabah) in order of inheritance.
D) Any Muslim adult relative.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, any agnate guardian may marry off a minor, who retains the right to annul the marriage at puberty.
